Source pages p. 548

Commentary
This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya deals with the rulings concerning a child (sabi) and a slave ('abd) who enter the state of ihram for Hajj before becoming fully obligated (mukallaf). The author first discusses an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding whether the obligation of Hajj is immediate (fawri) or delayed (taraakhi). According to Muhammad, the obligation is delayed on condition that it is not completely missed; if one performs it in subsequent years, it is considered performance by consensus, but if one dies without performing it, he is sinful by consensus. However, the nature of the sin differs: Abu Yusuf holds that sin is for the delay from the first year and is not lifted by later performance, while Muhammad holds that the sin is suspended and lifted upon performance. The practical consequence (thamarat al-khilaf) is that if one performs after the first year, Abu Yusuf considers him sinful for the delay, whereas Muhammad does not.

Then the passage addresses the case of a child entering ihram and then reaching puberty. The author clarifies that the child and slave have ahliyyat al-ada' (capacity for performance) but lack ahliyyat al-wujub (capacity for obligation), so their Hajj is valid as nafl (supererogatory) but does not fulfill the obligatory Hajj. If they later become obligated (by puberty or freedom), they must perform the obligatory Hajj. For the child, if he renews his ihram for the obligatory Hajj after his initial nafl ihram, and then stands at 'Arafah, it suffices ofr his obligatory Hajj, because his initial ihram was not binding (ghayr lazim) due to lack of capacity. In contrast, the slave's ihram is binding (lazim), so he cannot simply exit it and start anew; his performance during slavery counts as nafl, and after freedom he must perform a separate obligatory Hajj.

The passage then defines the obligations (fard) of Hajj as three: ihram, standing at 'Arafah, and Tawaf al-Ziyarah. It elaborates that ihram is a condition at the start but has the ruling of a pillar at the end, and standing at 'Arafah is the greatest pillar. Tawaf al-Ziyarah is also a pillar, performed on the day of Nahr or the two days after.

This passage is a typical example of Hanafi fiqh expansion, combining a matn ruling with its proof (dalil from the Prophet's delaying Hajj) and intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.

Key Terms
أهلية الوجوب (ahliyyat al-wujub): Legal capacity to have an obligation imposed; lacking in child and slave for Hajj.
أهلية الأداء (ahliyyat al-ada'): Capacity to perform actions validly; present in child and slave.
لازم (lazim): Binding; refers to an act once started cannot be exited except by completion.
غير لازم (ghayr lazim): Non-binding; the child's ihram can be broken.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the difference between a child and a slave who entered ihram before becoming obligated, in terms of renewing ihram after obligation?
A) The child can renew his ihram for the fard; the slave cannot.
B) The slave can renew his ihram; the child cannot.
C) Neither can renew; both must perform a fresh Hajj.
D) Both can renew because their ihram was nafl.

Key Takeaway
A child or slave's Hajj before obligation is valid nafl, not fard; after obligation, the child can renew ihram for fard, while the slave must perform a separate fard Hajj.

Hajj — Pilgrimage

The Fard of Hajj and the Ruling on a Minor or Slave
Explains the fard and wajib of Hajj and a specific ruling on minor/slave Hajj.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the three fard of Hajj, identify the wajibat, and explain the Hanafi ruling on a Hajj performed by a minor or slave before obligation, distinguishing between binding and non-binding ihram.

Core Idea
Hajj has three fard: ihram, standing at Arafah, and tawaf al-ziyarah; a child's Hajj becomes fard only if ihram is renewed after puberty, but a slave's Hajj remains nafl.

This lesson covers two essential aspects of Hajj: the obligatory components that define a valid Hajj, and the special ruling for those who perform Hajj before they are legally obligated. The Hanafi school carefully distinguishes between acts that are fard (pillars/conditions) and those that are wajib (compensable). It also addresses the scenario of a minor or slave who performs Hajj and later becomes qualified, offering a nuanced ruling based on the binding nature of ihram and legal capacity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three fard of Hajj
The text explicitly lists ihram, standing at Arafah, and tawaf al-ziyarah as the fard. These are essential elements without which the Hajj is invalid.
The term fard here includes both pillars (arkan) and a condition (shart) applied to ihram.
2
Understand ihram's dual nature
Ihram is a condition at the beginning (shart ibtida'an) — it can be performed before the months of Hajj — but it has the ruling of a pillar at the end (rukn intiha'an). If the standing at Arafah is missed, one cannot remain in ihram; they must exit with umrah and repeat Hajj later.
This dual nature is unique to the Hanafi school.
3
Define standing at Arafah and Tawaf al-Ziyarah
Standing at Arafah requires presence for even a moment on the 9th of Dhul-Hijjah and the night of the 10th. Tawaf al-Ziyarah is the circumambulation of the Ka'bah performed on the Day of Sacrifice or the two days after; four circuits constitute the rukn, the remaining three are wajib.
Standing at Arafah is the greatest pillar (rukn a'dham).
4
List the wajibat of Hajj
The text mentions wajibat: standing at Muzdalifah (Jam'), sa'y between Safa and Marwah, stoning the jamarat, tawaf al-sadr for the afaqi (one from outside the miqat), and shaving or trimming the hair. These are obligatory but their omission does not invalidate Hajj; they require a dam (sacrifice).
The author also notes that there are additional wajibat and that the list is not exhaustive.
5
Analyze the ruling on a minor or slave
If a boy or slave performs Hajj before being obligated (under puberty or in slavery), their Hajj is nafl. After they become obligated (puberty or freedom), they renew their ihram with the intention of fard Hajj. For the boy, this is possible because his initial ihram was not legally binding. For the slave, it is not possible because his ihram was binding. Additionally, renewal of ihram after standing at Arafah is ineffective because the main pillar has already been missed.
The key distinction is that the boy lacked legal capacity (ahliyyah), so his ihram was non-binding; the slave had capacity but lacked freedom, yet his ihram was binding.

Book of Hajj / Conditions of Hajj. Or a slave who is freed, then proceeds, he has not performed his obligatory Hajj. If the boy renews his ihram for the obligatory Hajj then stands (at Arafah), it is valid for him, unlike the slave; because the ihram of the boy was not binding due to lack of legal capacity, while the ihram of the slave is binding, so he cannot exit it by commencing something else. (2) And its fard: ihram, standing at Arafah, and Tawaf al-Ziyarah. After puberty and freedom, if they are able to afford provisions and other things, Hajj becomes obligatory upon them. If the boy reaches puberty or the slave is freed after having entered ihram in their state of childhood or slavery, and then they proceed and complete the rites of their Hajj, that does not count as obligatory, because their ihram was contracted for supererogatory (Hajj). If the boy breaks his ihram after puberty and renews his ihram for the obligatory Hajj, it is valid for him, unlike the slave. [1] His saying: 'stood' indicates that if he renews ihram after standing at Arafah, it does not suffice, because the greatest pillar of Hajj is standing at Arafah. (2) His saying: 'its fard' means what is made obligatory in Hajj, and the intent is more general than pillar (rukn) and condition (shart). (3) His saying: 'ihram' is intention of Hajj with the heart, accompanied by talbiyah or what stands in its place; it is a condition at the beginning, so it is permissible to perform it before the months of Hajj, though disliked, and it has the ruling of a pillar at the end, so that it is not permissible for one who misses Hajj to continue in it; rather he must end it with Umrah and make it up the following year. If it were purely a condition, it would be permissible to continue in it. Thus in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and others. [4] His saying: 'standing' and what follows are two pillars of Hajj, and by it is meant standing even for a moment on the ninth day of Dhul-Hijjah and the night of the tenth. 'Arafah is with fatha, also called 'Arafat: a place twelve miles from Makkah. [0] His saying: 'Tawaf al-Ziyarah' is circling the House on the Day of Sacrifice or the two days after. And its obligatory (wajib) items: standing at Jam' (Muzdalifah), Sa'y between Safa and Marwah, stoning the Jamarat, Tawaf al-Sadr for the one from outside the Miqat (afaqi), shaving (or trimming), and others are sunnahs and etiquettes. Its months are Shawwal, Dhul-Qa'dah, and the first ten days of Dhul-Hijjah; it is disliked to enter ihram for it before these months.
Source pages p. 549

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah combines a specific ruling with a general taxonomy of Hajj obligations. It opens with a ruling: if a boy or a slave performs Hajj before reaching puberty or being freed respectively, and then after becoming qualified (i.e., after puberty for the boy or freedom for the slave) they continue and complete the Hajj without renewing ihram, that Hajj does not count as the obligatory (fard) Hajj. The reason is that their initial ihram was for a supererogatory (nafl) Hajj, since they were not obligated at that time. The boy, however, has the option to break his ihram after puberty and renew it with the intention of fard Hajj; if he then stands at Arafah, it counts as his fard. The slave, by contrast, cannot do this because his ihram (having been valid at the time) is binding; he cannot annul it simply by intending something else. The author explains that if the boy renews his ihram after standing at Arafah, it does not suffice, because the main pillar (rukn a'dham) of Hajj is the standing at Arafah. Next, the passage lists the fard (obligatory) components of Hajj, understood broadly to include both pillars and conditions. The first is ihram, which is defined as the intention of Hajj in the heart along with talbiyah or its equivalent. Ihram is a condition at the beginning (shart ibtida'an), meaning one can enter it before the months of Hajj (though this is disliked), but at the end it takes the ruling of a pillar (rukn intiha'an), so if someone misses the standing at Arafah, they cannot remain in ihram; they must exit with a separate Umrah and make up the Hajj the following year. The second fard is standing at Arafah, which can be as brief as a moment on the 9th of Dhul-Hijjah and the night of the 10th, at a specific location about 12 miles from Makkah. The third fard is Tawaf al-Ziyarah, also called Tawaf al-Rukn or Tawaf al-Ifadah, performed on the Day of Sacrifice or the two days after; its pillar is completed with four circuits. The passage then lists the wajibat (obligatory but not fard) of Hajj, including standing at Muzdalifah (Jam'), Sa'y between Safa and Marwah, stoning the Jamarat, and Tawaf al-Sadr (farewell circumambulation) for the afaqi (one who entered from outside the Miqat). Shaving or trimming the hair is also mentioned as wajib in some views, but here it is listed under 'others' which the commentary notes include both sunnah and etiquettes. The months of Hajj are Shawwal, Dhul-Qa'dah, and the first ten days of Dhul-Hijjah; it is disliked to enter ihram before these months.

Key Terms
الفرض (al-fard): In this text, fard refers to the essential obligations of Hajj without which the Hajj is invalid; they include ihram, standing at Arafah, and tawaf al-ziyarah.
الوقف بعرفة (al-wuquf bi 'Arafah): The act of being present at the plain of Arafah on the 9th of Dhul-Hijjah, even for a moment; it is the greatest pillar of Hajj.
طواف الزيارة (tawaf al-ziyarah): The circumambulation of the Ka'bah performed on the Day of Sacrifice or the two days after; also called tawaf al-ifadah or tawaf al-rukun.
لازم (lazim): Binding or obligatory in the sense that it cannot be annulled; used to describe ihram that once entered, must be completed unless a valid exit (like umrah + qada) is performed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence of a slave's ihram being binding (lazim)?
A) The slave cannot exit his ihram even after being freed, so his Hajj remains nafl.
B) The slave's Hajj automatically becomes fard upon being freed.
C) The slave must renew his ihram after freedom for his Hajj to count.
D) The slave's ihram is invalid because he was not free.

Key Takeaway
Hajj has three fard (ihram, standing at Arafah, tawaf al-ziyarah) and several wajibat; a child's Hajj becomes fard only if he renews ihram after puberty before standing at Arafah, while a slave's Hajj remains nafl because his ihram was binding.

The Obligatory Acts and Months of Hajj in the Hanafi School
States the obligatory acts and months of Hajj.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the five wajibat of Hajj according to the Hanafi school and explain why Tawaf al-Sadr is not required for Makkans.

Core Idea
Hajj has five wajibat; the months are fixed, and ihram before them is makruh.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya lays out the essential obligations (wajibat) of Hajj and the specific months in which it must be performed. The Hanafi school distinguishes these wajibat from the sunan and pillars, and the commentator notes that the list is not exhaustive. Understanding these obligations is crucial for every pilgrim to ensure a valid Hajj without missing anything that requires compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the listed wajibat
Read the Arabic text and note the five acts explicitly named as wajib: standing at Jam' (Muzdalifah), Sa'i between Safa and Marwah, Rami al-Jimar, Tawaf al-Sadr (for the Aafaqi), and Halq (shaving or shortening hair).
These are the core obligatory acts; other acts are classified as sunan or adab.
2
Understand the exception for Tawaf al-Sadr
The text specifies 'for the Aafaqi'—someone from outside Makkah. The commentator explains that a Makkan does not need to perform Tawaf al-Sadr because they are not returning from Makkah to a distant homeland.
This shows how rulings can vary based on one's location.
3
Define the months of Hajj
The months are Shawwal, Dhu al-Qa'dah, and the first ten days of Dhu al-Hijjah. The proof is the Quranic verse. It is makruh to assume ihram for Hajj before these months.
The prohibition is because it resembles performing the pillar (standing at 'Arafat) outside its time.
4
Recognize the commentator's critique
The commentator notes that the author's list of wajibat is incomplete—other obligations exist (e.g., staying at Mina). He directs readers to Lubab al-Manasi for full details.
This emphasizes that this passage is a summary, not an exhaustive list.

And its obligations: Standing at Jam' (which is Muzdalifah), and Sa'i between Safa and Marwah, and Rami al-Jimar, and Tawaf al-Sadr for the Aafaqi, and Halq, and others are Sunan and Adab. And its months: Shawwal, Dhu al-Qa'dah, and the ten days of Dhu al-Hijjah. And it is disliked to put on Ihram for it before them. (1) Saying: Jam'; with fatha: is a place between Makkah and Mina, known as Muzdalifah from izlaf, meaning gathering. [2] Saying: al-Sadr; with two fatha: meaning the return from Makkah to his homeland. [3] Saying: for the Aafaqi; meaning one who enters Makkah from its horizons; i.e., its sides; as for the Makkan, he does not travel from Makkah after Hajj, so Tawaf al-Sadr is not obligatory upon him. [4] Saying: and others; meaning besides these mentioned, either emphasized Sunnah or recommended acts, but there is deficiency, for he did not encompass all the obligations, for there are other obligations as well, so his saying 'and others are Sunan and Adab' is not correct in its absoluteness. And if you wish details of these topics, refer to Lubab al-Manasi by Rahmatullah al-Sindi, and its commentary by Ali al-Qari. (0) Saying: and its months; meaning the months of Hajj mentioned in His saying: 'Hajj is in well-known months...' Whoever undertakes Hajj in them, then no sexual relations, nor disobedience, nor disputation during Hajj. Therefore, it is disliked to put on Ihram for Hajj before them, because it resembles the pillar.
Source pages p. 550

Commentary
This passage states the obligations (wajibat) of Hajj. According to the Hanafi school, the obligatory acts include standing at Muzdalifah (also called Jam'), sa'i between Safa and Marwah, stoning the jamarat, Tawaf al-Sadr (the farewell circumambulation) for those from outside Makkah, and shaving the head. The author lists other acts as sunnah or adab. The commentator notes that the author did not enumerate all wajibat, such as staying at Mina, and refers to Lubab al-Manasi for details. The months of Hajj are specified as Shawwal, Dhu al-Qa'dah, and the first ten days of Dhu al-Hijjah. It is disliked (makruh) to assume ihram for Hajj before these months because it resembles the pillar of standing at 'Arafat, which must occur within these months. The commentator defines the key terms: Jam' is Muzdalifah, Sadr means return, and Aafaqi is someone from outside the miqat. The proof for the months is the Quranic verse, and the dislike is based on analogy.

Key Terms
جمع (Jam'): A place between Makkah and Mina, also known as Muzdalifah; from izlaf meaning gathering.
صدر (Sadr): The return from Makkah to one's homeland.
آفاقي (Aafaqi): One who comes from beyond the miqat boundaries.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which of the following is considered a wajib of Hajj?
A) Standing at 'Arafat
B) Standing at Muzdalifah
C) Sacrifice
D) Hajj from miqat

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes five wajibat of Hajj: standing at Muzdalifah, Sa'i, Rami, Tawaf al-Sadr (for non-Makkans), and Halq; the months are fixed as Shawwal, Dhu al-Qa'dah, and first ten days of Dhu al-Hijjah; entering ihram before these months is makruh.

Rulings of Umrah in Hanafi Fiqh
States legal ruling, components, and disliked days of Umrah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling of Umrah, identify its pillars and conditions, and list the days when it is disliked.

Core Idea
Umrah is a confirmed Sunnah, not obligatory, and is disliked on the days of Hajj.

This lesson explains the Hanafi position on Umrah. While many Muslims consider Umrah a highly recommended act, its exact legal status differs between schools. We will examine the ruling, the essential acts, and the specific days when performing Umrah is disliked according to the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being discussed and what legal status (hukm) the Hanafi school assigns to it.
The passage states 'Wa al-Umrah sunnah' – Umrah is a Sunnah, meaning sunnah mu'akkadah.
2
List the pillars and conditions
Identify the essential components (arkan) and prerequisites (shurut) mentioned in the passage and commentary.
The passage mentions Tawaf and Sa'i as pillars; the commentary adds Ihram as a condition and shaving/trimming as required.
3
Determine the times of dislike
Identify the specific days when Umrah is disliked and understand why.
Disliked on the day of 'Arafah and four days after (10th-13th of Dhu al-Hijjah).
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Compare the Hanafi ruling with that of Imam al-Shafi'i, who considers Umrah wajib.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf; both schools have evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعمرة سنة : وهي طواف وسعي ، ولا وقوف لها"، وجازت في كل السنة، ، وكُرِهَت" في يوم عرفة وأربعة بعدها. (01) قوله : وهي طواف وسعي ؛ اقتصر على ذكر الركنين، ويشترط لهما الإحرام قبلهما، والحلق أو التقصير بعدهما. [٢] قوله : ولا وقوف لها ؛ أي ليس فيها وقوف بعرفة ولا مزدلفة ، ولا رواح إلى منى. And Umrah is a Sunnah: it is Tawaf and Sa'i, and there is no standing for it. And it is permissible in the entire year, but it is disliked on the day of 'Arafah and four days after it. (01) His saying: 'it is Tawaf and Sa'i'; he confined himself to mentioning the two pillars, and Ihram before them and shaving or trimming after them are required. [2] His saying: 'and there is no standing for it'; meaning there is no standing at 'Arafah or Muzdalifah, nor going to Mina. (3) His saying: 'and it is disliked'; meaning tahriman (prohibitively disliked), as stated in al-Fath. [4] His saying: 'and four days after it'; meaning the tenth, eleventh, twelfth, and thirteenth days, because these are the days of the actions of Hajj, so the actions of Umrah do not enter into them; therefore the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) commanded 'A'ishah (may Allah be pleased with her) to perform Umrah after completing Hajj, as established in the Sahihain and others.
Source pages p. 551

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on Umrah. Umrah is a confirmed Sunnah (sunnah mu'akkadah), not obligatory. Its essential acts (arkan) are Tawaf and Sa'i, performed after assuming Ihram. The commentator adds that Ihram before Tawaf and Sa'i is a condition, and shaving or trimming hair is required after them. Umrah may be performed at any time of year, but it is prohibitively disliked (makruh tahriman) on the day of 'Arafah and the four days following it (10th-13th of Dhu al-Hijjah) because these are the days of Hajj rituals. The prophetic precedent of 'A'ishah's Umrah after Hajj is cited as evidence. The commentator specifically notes that the dislike is tahriman, based on al-Fath al-Qadir.

Key Terms
عمرة (Umrah): A pilgrimage to Makkah consisting of Tawaf and Sa'i without standing at 'Arafah.
سنة (Sunnah): Recommended but not obligatory; in Hanafi usage, between mubah and wajib.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The day of 'Arafah only
B) The day of 'Arafah and four days after
C) The first ten days of Dhul Hijjah
D) The days of Tashriq (11th-13th of Dhul Hijjah)

Key Takeaway
Umrah is a confirmed Sunnah, performed by Tawaf and Sa'i, and is prohibitively disliked on the day of 'Arafah and the following four days.

The Mawaqit: Places of Ihram for Madani and Iraqi Pilgrims
Specifies miqat for Madani and Iraqi pilgrims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the specific miqat for pilgrims arriving from Madinah and Iraq and explain the legal basis for these mawaqit.

Core Idea
The miqat for a Madani pilgrim is Dhu al-Hulayfah; for an Iraqi pilgrim it is Dhat 'Irq — established by prophetic Sunnah.

This lesson clarifies the designated places (mawaqit) where pilgrims from Madinah and Iraq must enter the state of ihram. Understanding these locations is essential for performing Hajj or Umrah correctly according to the Hanafi school, as passing them without ihram incurs a penalty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two groups
The passage mentions two categories: the Madani (coming from Madinah) and the Iraqi (coming from Iraq). Note the linguistic forms and what they refer to.
Madani is with two fatha, meaning 'related to Madinah'.
2
Locate the assigned miqat
For each group, the text explicitly states the miqat: Dhu al-Hulayfah for the Madani, and Dhat 'Irq for the Iraqi. Memorize these names and their approximate distances.
Dhu al-Hulayfah is about 6-7 miles from Madinah; Dhat 'Irq is about 42 miles from Makkah.
3
Understand the definition of miqat
The commentary defines miqat as the place from which the Aafaqi (one from outside the miqat zone) assumes ihram for Hajj or Umrah. This is a technical term in fiqh.
The miqat applies to both Hajj and Umrah, not just Hajj.
4
Appreciate the prophetic evidence
The passage notes that these mawaqit are established by the Prophet (peace be upon him) in the Sahihain and Sunan. This gives them authority in the Hanafi school.
These are unanimous among the schools.

Commentary
This passage specifies the miqat (place of entering ihram) for two categories of pilgrims: the Madani (one coming from Madinah) must assume ihram at Dhu al-Hulayfah (known today as Abyar 'Ali), and the Iraqi at Dhat 'Irq. The commentator defines miqat as the place where one from outside the miqat boundaries (afaqi) must enter ihram. These mawaqit are established by the Prophet's practice, recorded in authentic Hadith collections. The commentator also gives distances: Dhu al-Hulayfah is about 6-7 miles from Madinah, while Dhat 'Irq is about 42 miles from Makkah. These specific locations are part of the Hanafi school's reliance on the Sunnah for determining miqat.

Key Terms
ميقات (Miqat): The specific place from which a pilgrim must assume ihram.
مدني (Madani): One who comes from Madinah to perform Hajj or Umrah.
ذو الحليفة (Dhu al-Hulayfah): A place near Madinah, the miqat for Madani pilgrims; also known as Abyar 'Ali.
ذات عرق (Dhat 'Irq): A place approximately 42 miles from Makkah, the miqat for Iraqi pilgrims.

The Mawaqit and the Ruling on Passing Them
Lists the mawaqit and the ruling on delaying/advancing ihram

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five mawaqit for different regions and explain the Hanafi ruling on delaying or advancing ihram.

Core Idea
The miqat is a fixed station: delaying ihram beyond it is prohibitively disliked, but advancing is permissible.

This lesson covers the designated locations (mawaqit) for entering ihram when traveling to Makkah for Hajj or 'Umrah. The Hanafi school, relying on Prophetic hadith, holds that passing these points without ihram is prohibited, though the prohibition is at the level of makruh tahrimi. However, assuming ihram before reaching the miqat is perfectly valid and even recommended by some.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mawaqit and their regions
List each miqat mentioned: Dhu al-Hulayfah (Madinah), Dhat 'Irq (Iraq), Juhfah (Sham), Qarn (Najd), and Yalamlam (Yemen). Note the approximate distances from either Madinah or Makkah as given.
These are the five famous mawaqit; the Hanafi school accepts them without dispute.
2
Understand the key ruling on delaying and advancing ihram
The text states 'Hurm' (forbidden) to delay ihram beyond the miqat, but the commentator clarifies it is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) because the proof is a hadith ahad. Advancing ihram before the miqat is explicitly permitted.
The distinction between haram and makruh tahrimi depends on the strength of the evidence.
3
Analyze the proof (dalil)
The hadith 'Do not pass the miqat except with ihram' (narrated by Ibn Abi Shaybah) is the primary evidence. In Hanafi usul, a prohibition (nahy) from an ahad hadith indicates makruh tahrimi, not absolute haram.
The evidence is zanni in transmission (thubut) and meaning (dalala).
4
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Consider a pilgrim from Madinah who reaches Dhu al-Hulayfah but does not assume ihram until after passing it. The ruling: the act is makruh tahrimi, and the pilgrim may need to return to the miqat or offer a dam (sacrifice) according to some views.
If the pilgrim assumes ihram before Dhu al-Hulayfah, it is valid and permissible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وميقات المدني : ذو الحليفة، والعراقي ذات عرق، والشامي جحفة، والنجدي قرن، واليمني يلملم، وحرم تأخير الإحرام عنها لمن قصد دخول مكة لا التقديم. قوله : وميقات؛ هو الموضع الذي يحرم الآفاقي للحج أو العمرة منها، وقد ثبت عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم التوقيت بهذه المواقيت في «الصحيحين» و«السنن». قوله : المدني؛ بفتحتين: نسبة إلى المدينة النبوية، والمراد به من يأتي من المدينة إلى مكة. قوله : ذو الحليفة؛ بضم الحاء، اسم موضع بينه وبين المدينة ستة أميال أو سبعة. قوله : عرق؛ بكسر العين؛ اسم موضع بينها وبين مكة اثنان وأربعون ميلاً. قوله : جحفة؛ بضم الجيم، اسم موضع بينه وبين مكة ثلاث مراحل أو أكثر. قوله : والنجدي؛ أي من يأتي من بلاد نجد ميقاته قرن، وهو بالفتح: اسم موضع بينه وبين مكة اثنان وأربعون ميلاً، وقيل: غير ذلك. قوله : واليمني؛ بفتحتين؛ أي من يأتي من بلاد اليمن ميقاته: يلملم، بفتح الياء المثناة التحتية، وفتح اللامين بينهما ميم ساكنة: اسم جبل بينه وبين مكة مرحلتان. قوله : وحرم؛ أي كره تحريماً؛ لحديث: «لا تجاوزوا الميقات إلا بإحرام» أخرجه ابن أبي شيبة. قوله : لا التقديم؛ أي تقديم الإحرام على المواقيت، وقد ثبت في ذلك أخبار وآثار من الصحابة بسطناها في «التعليق المجد على موطأ محمد».
And the miqat of the Madani is Dhu al-Hulayfah, and of the Iraqi is Dhat 'Irq, and of the Sham'i is Juhfah, and of the Najdi is Qarn, and of the Yamani is Yalamlam. And it is forbidden to delay the ihram from them for one who intends to enter Makkah, but not to advance it. He said: 'Miqat'; it is the place from which the afaqi enters into ihram for Hajj or 'Umrah, and the timing of these mawaqit has been established from the Messenger of Allah صلى الله عليه وسلم in al-Sahihayn and al-Sunan. He said: 'al-Yamani'; with two fathahs, meaning the one who comes from the land of Yemen, his miqat is Yalamlam, with a fatha on the ya', and fatha on the two lams with a sukun on the mim, a mountain between it and Makkah two stages. He said: 'and it is forbidden'; i.e. prohibitively disliked, due to the hadith: 'Do not pass the miqat except with ihram' narrated by Ibn Abi Shaybah. He said: 'not the advancement'; meaning advancing ihram before the mawaqit, and there have been established regarding that reports and athar from the Companions which we have detailed in al-Ta'liq al-Mujadd 'ala Muwatta' Muhammad.

Commentary
This passage details the mawaqit—the designated places for entering into ihram for Hajj or 'Umrah. The matn lists them by region: for those coming from Madinah, the miqat is Dhu al-Hulayfah (also known as Abyar 'Ali); for Iraq, Dhat 'Irq; for Syria (Sham), Juhfah (now often from Rabigh); for Najd, Qarn al-Manazil; and for Yemen, Yalamlam. The commentator explains each location's distance. The key ruling is that it is forbidden (actually makruh tahrimi) to delay ihram beyond these points for anyone intending to enter Makkah, meaning one must assume ihram before crossing the miqat. However, it is perfectly permissible to advance the ihram—i.e., assume ihram before reaching the miqat—based on reports from the Companions. The proof for the prohibition of delaying is the hadith 'Do not pass the miqat except with ihram' narrated by Ibn Abi Shaybah. This ruling applies to all pilgrims (afaqi) coming from outside the Haram. The mawaqit are fixed by the Prophet's sunnah as recorded in the authentic collections.

Key Terms
ميقات (miqat): The specific location from which a pilgrim must assume ihram for Hajj or 'Umrah.
ذو الحليفة (Dhu al-Hulayfah): The miqat for pilgrims coming from Madinah, located about 6-7 miles from Madinah.
أفقي (afaqi): One who comes from outside the miqat boundaries (i.e., not a resident of the Haram).

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Wajib (obligatory)

Key Takeaway
The five mawaqit are fixed by sunnah; delaying ihram beyond any of them is makruh tahrimi, but advancing it is permissible.

The Miqat for Meccan Residents and Those Inside the Miqat
Defining miqat for Hajj and Umrah for two categories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for the miqat of a Meccan resident for Hajj and Umrah, and identify the scriptural proofs for each.

Core Idea
For Meccan residents: Hajj ihram from the Haram, Umrah ihram from the Hal — each based on distinct hadith evidence.

The miqat (boundary for entering ihram) varies depending on one's location relative to Mecca. This passage clarifies the specific rulings for two groups: (1) those who live within the outer miqat but outside Mecca, and (2) residents of Mecca itself. Understanding these distinctions is essential for correct Hajj and Umrah performance, and the text supports each ruling with clear prophetic traditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The passage addresses two groups: (a) those who live inside the outer miqat but outside Mecca, and (b) those who reside in Mecca.
2
State the ruling for the first group
For group (a), the miqat is the Hal (area outside the Haram). They may enter Mecca without ihram for general purposes, but for Hajj/Umrah they must assume ihram from the Hal.
The proof is the hadith of Ibn Umar from Muwatta.
3
State the ruling for Meccan residents
For group (b), the miqat differs: for Hajj it is the Haram; for Umrah it is the Hal. The passage gives the reasoning: Hajj's major ritual (wuquf at Arafat) is in the Hal, so ihram from the Haram suffices; Umrah's rituals are inside the Haram, so a short journey from the Hal is required.
4
Identify the proofs for Meccan Hajj
The proof for Hajj is the Prophet's command to his companions during the Farewell Pilgrimage to assume ihram from the Haram and proceed to Mina and Arafat.
This is recorded in Sahih Bukhari and Muslim.
5
Identify the proofs for Meccan Umrah
The proof for Umrah is the Prophet's instruction to Aisha to go to At-Tan'im (in the Hal) to assume ihram for Umrah. Additional evidence comes from Abu Hurairah's interpretation of the Quranic verse (2:196) requiring ihram from one's own dwelling.
These are ahad hadith, but sufficient for establishing the ruling.

Book of Hajj / Conditions of Hajj. 553. And it is permissible for those of its interior [i.e., those living inside the miqat] to enter Mecca without being in ihram; so their miqat is the Hal (the area outside the Haram). And for whoever resides in Mecca: for Hajj, [their miqat is] the Haram; for Umrah, the Hal. "And it is permissible for those of its interior" (i.e., entering Mecca without ihram) (18): "so their miqat is the Hal" means: whoever is inside the miqat but outside Mecca, his miqat is the Hal, i.e., outside the Haram. (And for whoever resides in Mecca: for Hajj the Haram, and for Umrah the Hal) because Hajj is at Arafat, which is in the Hal, so his ihram is from the Haram, and Umrah is in the Haram, so his ihram is from the Hal to achieve a type of travel. [1] His saying: "for those of its interior" means whoever is between him and Mecca. [2] His saying: "without ihram" is established from Ibn Umar, recorded by Malik in Al-Muwatta. (3) His saying: "so his miqat" means if he intends ihram for Hajj or Umrah while he is between Mecca and the miqat. [4] His saying: "and for whoever" whether he is a Meccan or an Afaqi (outsider) who resides there. [0] His saying: "the Haram" means he assumes ihram from the Haram, then goes out to Mina and Arafat; the Messenger of Allah commanded his companions to do this during the Farewell Pilgrimage, and he commanded Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) to assume ihram for Umrah from At-Tan'im, which is outside the Haram; all this is recorded in the Sahihs and Sunan. (2) And from Abu Hurairah (may Allah be pleased with him) regarding His statement: "And complete the Hajj and Umrah for Allah" [Al-Baqarah 2:196], he said: "Part of the completion of Hajj is that you assume ihram from the dwelling of your family." [Recorded in] Sunan Al-Bayhaqi Al-Kabir (5:30) and Shu'ab Al-Iman (3:447); Al-Suyuti declared it hasan in Al-Jami' Al-Saghir (2:537); Al-Tahanawi said in I'la Al-Sunan (10:26): "Perhaps his hasan rating is due to supporting evidence." And from him regarding the verse, he said: "That you assume ihram from the dwelling of your family." [Recorded in] Al-Mustadrak (2:303) and he declared it sahih; and Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah (3:125), Sharh Ma'ani Al-Athar (2:159), Al-Athar (1:101), Al-Ahadith Al-Mukhtara (2:221), and he said: "Its chain is sahih." (1) Because he frequently enters it for need, and requiring ihram every time would be burdensome. Refer to Sharh Ibn Malik (folio 66/a). (2) From Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her): I arrived in Mecca while menstruating and did not perform tawaf of the House or between Safa and Marwa. I complained to the Messenger of Allah, and he said: "Undo your hair, comb it, and declare ihram for Hajj, and leave the Umrah." She said: I did so. When we completed the Hajj, the Messenger of Allah sent me with Abdur Rahman bin Abi Bakr to At-Tan'im, and I performed Umrah. [Recorded in] Sahih Muslim (2:570), Sahih Al-Bukhari (2:563), and others.
Source pages p. 553

Commentary
This passage from Imam Abd al-Barr's commentary on Al-Wiqayah addresses the specific rulings for the miqat (the boundary for entering the state of ihram) for two groups: (1) those who live within the general miqat (i.e., the five designated places for outsiders) but outside Mecca, and (2) those who reside in Mecca itself. For the first group, the text establishes that they may enter Mecca without ihram for ordinary purposes, but if they intend Hajj or Umrah, their miqat is the 'Hal' — meaning the area surrounding the Haram but outside its boundaries. This is because they are already inside the outer miqat, so ihram must be taken from the next boundary, which is the Hal. The ruling is supported by a narration from Ibn Umar recorded in Malik's Muwatta. For the second group — residents of Mecca — the rule differentiates between Hajj and Umrah. For Hajj, they assume ihram from the Haram itself (i.e., from within Mecca), because the key ritual of wuquf at Arafat occurs in the Hal, so they commute from the Haram to Arafat. For Umrah, they must go to the Hal (e.g., At-Tan'im) to assume ihram, because Umrah's main rituals (tawaf and sa'i) are within the Haram, so a short journey from Hal to Haram fulfills the spirit of ihram from outside. The text cites two major proofs: the Prophet's command to his companions during the Farewell Pilgrimage to assume ihram from the Haram for Hajj, and his specific instruction to Aisha to go to At-Tan'im for Umrah. Additionally, a hadith from Abu Hurairah interprets the Quranic command to 'complete Hajj and Umrah' as requiring ihram from one's own home district, emphasizing the principle of commencing the pilgrimage journey from the miqat appropriate to one's location. The commentary clarifies that the reason for not requiring ihram from the outer miqat for those living inside is to avoid hardship, as they frequently enter Mecca for daily needs.

Key Terms
ميقات (miqat): The designated boundary or station where one must assume ihram for Hajj or Umrah. It can refer to the outer limits for those coming from afar or the inner boundaries for those already near Mecca.
الحل (al-hal): The area outside the sacred precincts of Mecca (al-Haram) but within the outer miqat boundaries. It is the miqat for those living inside the outer miqat but outside Mecca.
الحرم (al-haram): The sacred sanctuary of Mecca, including the Kaaba and the city itself. For Hajj, residents of Mecca assume ihram from within the Haram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Where must a person who lives in Mecca assume ihram for Umrah, according to this passage?
A) From the Haram (inside Mecca)
B) From the Hal (outside the Haram)
C) From the outer miqat (e.g., Dhul Hulaifa)
D) From their own home within Mecca

Key Takeaway
The miqat for a Meccan resident differs for Hajj and Umrah: Hajj ihram from the Haram, Umrah ihram from the Hal, based on the location of the main rituals and the principle of avoiding hardship.

Recommended Acts Before Ihram: Wudu, Ghusl, Garments, and Perfume
Commentary on recommended acts before Ihram with hadith proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four recommended acts before Ihram according to the Hanafi school, identify the supporting hadith proofs, and explain the legal status (sunnah) and conditions for each.

Core Idea
Ghusl and perfume for Ihram are sunnah, proven by prophetic practice, but not obligatory.

Before entering the state of Ihram, the Prophet (peace be upon him) would perform certain acts of preparation. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the base text's rulings and supports them with authentic hadith. Understanding the basis of these recommendations helps distinguish between what is required and what is virtuous.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four recommended acts
From the matn: perform wudu, ghusl (bath), wear two garments (izar and rida), and apply perfume. Each has a ruling of 'ahabba' (beloved/recommended).
Ghusl is emphasized as mustahabb; the term 'ahabba' indicates sunnah.
2
Locate the proof for each act
For ghusl: hadith from Zaid ibn Thabit and Jabir. For garments: hadith from Ibn Abbas. For perfume: hadith from Aisha and the story of the man at Ji'ranah. These show the Prophet's words and actions.
The commentator cites specific hadith collections.
3
Determine the hukm level and rationale
All four are sunnah (recommended), not fard. The commentator clarifies ghusl is for cleanliness (nazafah), not ritual purification (taharah). Therefore, menstruating women and children are also encouraged.
The rationale affects who is included; since it's for cleanliness, it is recommended for all.
4
Examine conditions and exceptions
For garments: the minimum is one garment, best is two. The only condition is avoidance of sewn garments (makhit). For perfume: it is sunnah to apply before Ihram, but the perfume must be washed off if applied after Ihram (as per the Ji'ranah hadith). Also, it is better not to perfume the garment itself.
The condition on garments is negative (avoid sewn). The perfume ruling includes a remedial action if done incorrectly.

Chapter of Ihram. (And whoever intends his Ihram, he performs wudu, and his bath is beloved, and he wears a loincloth and a cloak that are pure, and he applies perfume.) [1] His statement: 'beloved' meaning recommended due to its establishment from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) in words and deeds, in Jami' al-Tirmidhi and others, and it is for cleanliness not for ritual purity, so even the menstruating woman, the woman in postpartum bleeding, and the child are commanded to do it. (2) His statement: 'loincloth and cloak', this is the explanation of the least preferred, otherwise if he suffices with one, or wears more, it is permissible, and the condition is avoidance of sewn garments. [3] His statement: 'and he applies perfume' meaning he uses perfume following the example of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) when he applied perfume at his Ihram, reported by Malik and others. (1) From Zaid ibn Thabit (may Allah be pleased with him): that he saw the Prophet (peace be upon him) strip for his talbiyah and bathe in Sahih Ibn Khuzaimah (4:161) and al-Mustadrak (2:421) and Sunan al-Tirmidhi (3:192). And from Jabir (may Allah be pleased with him) that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said to Asma' bint 'Umais when she gave birth: 'Bathe and wrap yourself in a cloth and enter Ihram' in Sahih Muslim (2:887) and Sahih Ibn Khuzaimah (1:123) and others. (2) From Ibn 'Abbas (may Allah be pleased with him), he said: 'The Prophet (peace be upon him) set out from Madinah after he combed his hair, applied oil, and wore his loincloth and cloak, he and his Companions, and he did not forbid any of the cloaks and loincloths that are worn except the ones dyed with saffron that leave a mark on the skin' in Sahih al-Bukhari (2:560) and others. (3) And it is recommended that it be with musk and removing its substance with rose water or similar pure water, and it is better that he not perfume his garment. From 'A'ishah (may Allah be pleased with her), she said: 'I used to perfume the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) for his Ihram before he entered Ihram, and for his halal before he performed tawaf of the House' in Sahih Muslim (2:846) and Sahih al-Bukhari (1:104). ... And upon him a woolen cloak. And from Abu Ya'la (may Allah be pleased with him), he said: 'A man came to the Prophet (peace be upon him) while he was at Ji'ranah, smeared with perfume, and said: O Messenger of Allah, what do you think about a man who entered Ihram for 'Umrah in a cloak after smearing himself with perfume... So the Prophet said: As for the perfume on you, wash it three times, and as for the cloak, take it off, then do in your 'Umrah what you do in your Hajj' in Sahih Muslim (2:837) and Sahih al-Bukhari (2:557).
Source pages p. 554

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) of the matn rulings on the recommended acts before entering Ihram. The author first cites the base text: 'Whoever intends his Ihram, he performs wudu, his bath is beloved, he wears a clean loincloth and cloak, and applies perfume.' Then the commentator explains each part. The term 'beloved' (ahabba) means recommended (mustahabb), supported by hadith from Zaid ibn Thabit and Jabir regarding ghusl for Ihram. The commentator clarifies that ghusl is for cleanliness, not ritual purity, so it is recommended even for menstruating women and children. For clothing, the minimum is one garment, but the best is two (izar and rida). The prohibition is only against sewn garments (makhit). Perfume is recommended following the Prophet's example, with guidance that it should be musk, and the substance removed with rose water. The better view is not to perfume the garment, as shown by 'A'ishah's hadith. The final hadith about a man in a cloak (jubbah) with perfume shows the ruling: remove the perfume by washing and remove the sewn garment. This entire passage is from the Hanafi tradition, relying on these hadith proofs.

Key Terms
الإحرام (al-ihram): The state of ritual consecration for Hajj or Umrah, marked by intention and prohibition of certain actions.
الغسل (al-ghusl): The full body wash recommended before Ihram for cleanliness.
الإزار والرداء (al-izar wa al-rida): Two white, unsewn garments: the izar wrapped around the lower body, the rida over the upper body.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, why is ghusl recommended even for menstruating women and those in postpartum bleeding?
A) Because it removes ritual impurity (janabah).
B) Because it is for cleanliness (nazafah).
C) Because it is an obligatory act (fard).
D) Because it is a condition for the validity of Ihram.

Key Takeaway
The recommended acts before Ihram are sunnah, proven by the Prophet's practice, and the key condition for garments is avoiding sewn material.

Hajj — Pilgrimage
beginner
6 min
Recommended Acts Before Entering Ihram
Lists recommended pre-ihram acts with evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wudu, ghusl, garment, and perfume before ihram, and distinguish between recommended acts and the obligatory condition of avoiding stitched garments.

Core Idea
Before ihram, wudu, ghusl, izār and ridā’, and perfume are recommended; the only obligation is to avoid stitched garments.

The Hajj begins with ihram, a sacred state. The Hanafi school has clear guidelines on what to do before entering ihram and what conditions must be met. This lesson unpacks the classical commentary on al-Wiqaya, explaining the ruling for each recommended act and the critical condition of avoiding stitched clothing.

Guided Path
1
Identify each recommended act
List the four acts mentioned: performing wudu, performing ghusl (more beloved), wearing an izār and ridā’, and applying perfume.
The text uses 'ahabb' (more beloved) for ghusl, indicating a higher recommendation than wudu.
2
Determine the hukm level
For each act, classify its ruling: wudu, ghusl, and perfume are mustahabb (sunnah); wearing izār and ridā’ is also sunnah but the minimum is to cover properly.
The commentary explicitly states wudu is mustahabb for cleanliness, not purity.
3
Identify the condition
Note the phrase: 'the condition is to avoid stitched garments (makhīṭ)'. This is a shart (condition) for the validity of ihram, not a recommended act.
Stitched garments are prohibited for men in ihram; violation requires removal but does not nullify the intention.
4
Examine the evidence
The passage cites multiple hadith: ghusl from Zayd ibn Thabit, wearing izār from Ibn Abbas, perfume from Aisha, and the man in the jubbah from Abu Ya'la.
These are ahad hadith, establishing recommendation (istihbab) in Hanafi usul.
5
Conclude the fatwa position
Combine the rulings: the recommended acts are sunnah muakkadah; the only obligatory condition is that men must avoid stitched garments. Women are exempt from this garment condition.
This is the mufta bihi in the Hanafi school.

وعليه جبة صوف وعن أبي يعلى الله ، قال : أتى النبي رجل وهو بالجعرانة ، مُتَضَمِّخٌ بطيب، فقال يا رسول الله : كيف ترى في رجل أحرم بعمرة في جُبَّة بعدما تَضَمَّخ بطيب ... فقال النبي : أما الطيب الذي بك فاغسله ثلاث مرات ، وأما الجبة فانزعها ، ثم اصنع في عمرتك ما تصنع في حجّك» في صحيح مسلم (۲) : (۸۳۷)، و«صحيح البخاري» (٢ : ٥٥٧)
Chapter of Ihram. (And whoever intends his ihram performs wudu, and his ghusl is more beloved, and he wears an izār and a ridā’ that are pure, and applies perfume. [1] His saying: 'more beloved' means recommended due to its confirmation from the Messenger of Allah ﷺ in word and deed, in Jami' al-Tirmidhi and others, and it is for cleanliness not for purity, so even a menstruating woman, a postpartum woman, and a boy are commanded to do it. (2) His saying: 'izār and ridā’’ (3) this is a clarification of the minimum that is best, otherwise if he suffices with one, or wears more, it is permissible, and the condition is to avoid stitched garments. [3] His saying: 'and applies perfume' means he uses perfume following the Messenger of Allah ﷺ who applied perfume at his ihram (3), narrated by Malik and others. (1) From Zayd ibn Thabit  that he saw the Prophet  remove his clothes for ihram and perform ghusl (in Sahih Ibn Khuzaymah 4:161, al-Mustadrak 2:421, Sunan al-Tirmidhi 3:192). And from Jabir  that the Prophet  said to Asma bint 'Umays when she gave birth: 'Perform ghusl and wrap yourself in a cloth and enter ihram' (in Sahih Muslim 2:887, Sahih Ibn Khuzaymah 1:123, etc.). (2) From Ibn 'Abbas  who said: The Prophet  set out from Medina after combing his hair, applying oil, wearing his izār and ridā’ along with his Companions, and he did not forbid any type of wraps and izārs to be worn except those dyed with saffron that stain the skin (in Sahih al-Bukhari 2:560, etc.). (3) It is recommended that the perfume be musk and that its substance be removed with rose water or similar clean water, and it is better not to perfume his garment. From 'A'ishah  who said: 'I used to perfume the Messenger of Allah ﷺ for his ihram before he would enter ihram, and for his tahlil before he would circumambulate the House' (in Sahih Muslim 2:846, Sahih al-Bukhari 1:104). And he had on a woolen jubbah. And from Abu Ya'la  who said: A man came to the Prophet  at al-Ji'ranah, smeared with perfume, and said: O Messenger of Allah, what do you think of a man who entered ihram for 'umrah in a jubbah after applying perfume… The Prophet  said: 'As for the perfume on you, wash it off three times, and as for the jubbah, remove it, then do in your 'umrah what you do in your hajj' (in Sahih Muslim 2:837, Sahih al-Bukhari 2:557).
Source pages p. 555

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the recommended acts before entering ihram. The commentator (author of 'Umda al-Ri'aya) explains the matn (text) of al-Wiqaya. The ruling: wudu is mustahabb (recommended), not obligatory, because it is for cleanliness and not for ritual purity; hence even a menstruating woman, postpartum woman, or boy is encouraged to do it. Ghusl is 'more beloved' (ahabbu) — a higher recommendation. Wearing an izār (waist-wrapper) and ridā’ (upper garment) is the best minimum; one may wear only one or more, but the key condition is to avoid stitched garments (makhīṭ). Perfume is recommended based on the Prophet's own practice, but it should be applied to the body, not to the clothing, as the hadith of 'A'ishah indicates. The hadith of the man in the jubbah shows that if one enters ihram in a stitched garment with perfume, he must remove the garment and wash off the perfume. Overall, these are sunan (recommended acts) before ihram, not obligatory.

Key Terms
إزار (izār): A waist-wrapper, lower garment worn during ihram.
رداء (ridā’): An upper garment or cloak worn over the shoulders during ihram.
مخيط (makhīṭ): Stitched garments that are sewn to fit the body; wearing them is prohibited for a muhrim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following acts before ihram is considered mustahabb (recommended) rather than obligatory?
A) Performing wudu
B) Wearing a stitched garment
C) Reciting the talbiyah
D) Making the intention for hajj

Key Takeaway
The recommended acts before ihram are sunnah; the only obligatory condition is for men to avoid stitched garments.

Prayer and Talbiyah for the Mufrid bil-Hajj
Describes the supererogatory prayer and talbiyah for the mufrid bil-hajj.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the two-rak'ah prayer and talbiyah for the mufrid bil-hajj, and identify the prophetic evidence (dalil) for each sunnah.

Core Idea
The two-rak'ah prayer and the talbiyah before ihram are sunnah, not obligatory, and additions to the talbiyah are permissible.

Before entering the state of ihram, the pilgrim who intends only hajj (mufrid bil-hajj) is encouraged to perform two rak'ahs of supererogatory prayer, following the Prophet's practice at Dhu al-Hulayfah. Then, after a brief supplication, he recites the talbiyah—a formula of response to Allah's call. This lesson unpacks the rulings and proofs for these acts, clarifying their recommended nature and the scope for additional wording.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two acts described
Recognize that the passage covers (1) praying two rak'ahs (shaf'an) and (2) reciting the talbiyah after a supplication. These are the two sunan for the mufrid bil-hajj at the miqat.
The prayer is supererogatory (nafl), not obligatory.
2
State the ruling for each act
From the Hanafi position: both are sunnah mu'akkadah (emphasized recommended). The text says 'yusalli shaf'an' (he prays two) without commanding, and the talbiyah is introduced with 'then he recites talbiyah'—indicating recommendation. The commentary clarifies that decreasing the wording is discouraged (makruh tanzihi) but increasing is permissible.
Other schools differ: some consider talbiyah wajib.
3
Locate the dalil for each act
The passage cites hadiths: for the prayer, the action of the Prophet at Dhu al-Hulayfah (Ibn 'Umar, Ibn 'Abbas). For the talbiyah, the wording transmitted from the Prophet (Ibn 'Umar). These are ahad hadiths, zanni al-thubut and zanni al-dalalah, thus yielding sunnah, not fard.
The proofs are from fi'l (action) and qawl (saying) of the Prophet.
4
Analyse the ikhtilaf and mufta bihi
Note that some schools hold the talbiyah as wajib. The Hanafi school maintains sunnah due to the zanni evidence. The mufta bihi is that both are sunnah mu'akkadah, and adding to the talbiyah is permissible (ja'iz).
The cross-madhhab difference affects whether a pilgrim who omits the talbiyah must offer a dam (compensatory sacrifice) or not.

وقال المفرد بالحج : اللهم إني أريدُ الحج فيسره لي، وتقبله منِّي ، ثُمَّ لَبى ينوي به الحج، وهي : لبيك اللهم لبيك، لبيك لا شريك لك لبيك، إنَّ الحمد والنعمة لك والملك ، لا شريك لك ، ولا يُنْقِصُ منها ، وإن زاد جاز.
[ 1 ] قوله : شفعاً ، بالفتح ؛ أي صلى ركعتين تطوعاً. كذا ثبت عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم حين أحرم من ذي الحليفة(1).
(٢) قوله : المفرد بالحج ؛ أي من يحرم بالحج فقط من الميقات، ويفرده دون العمرة، وهذا الدعاء ونحوه بلسانه مستحب.
(۳) قوله : ثم لبى ؛ أي فأدّى بالتلبية سراً أو جهراً، أو الثاني أفضل. [٤] قوله : لبيك اللهم لبيك ؛ أي قمتُ ببابك إقامة بعد أخرى، وأجبت ندائكَ مرة بعد أخرى، وجملة اللهم معترضة بين المؤكد والمؤكد ، وقوله : إن بالكسر ويجوز الفتح.

وعن ابن عباس : خرج رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم حاجاً فلما صلى في مسجده بذي الحليفة ركعتيه، أوجبه في مجلسه، فأهل بالحج حين فرغ من ركعتيه» في «المستدرك»(١ : ٦٢٠)، وصححه، وسنن البيهقي الكبير (٥) : (۳۷) ، و (سنن أبي داود»(٢ : ١٥٠)، و(مسند أحمد (۱ : ٢٦٠)، وغيرها.
(۲) فعن ابن عمر : إن تلبية رسول الله له : لبيك اللهم لبيك ، لبيك لا شريك لك لبيك إن الحمد والنعمة لك والملك لا شريك لك ، وكان ابن عمر يزيد فيها : لبيك لبيك وسعديك والخير بيديك لبيك والرغباء إليك والعمل في صحيح مسلم (٢ : ٨٤١).
And he prays two rak'ahs. And the one performing hajj alone (mufrid bil-hajj) says: 'O Allah, I intend hajj, so make it easy for me and accept it from me.' Then he recites the talbiyah intending hajj with it, and it is: 'Labbayk Allahumma labbayk, labbayka la sharika laka labbayk, inna al-hamda wa al-ni'mata laka wa al-mulk, la sharika lak.' And he should not decrease it, and if he increases, it is permissible. [1] His saying: 'shaf'ān' (with fathah) means he prays two rak'ahs voluntarily. This is established from the Messenger of Allah ﷺ when he entered ihram from Dhu al-Hulayfah(1). (2) His saying: 'the mufrid bil-hajj' means one who enters ihram for hajj only from the miqat, and singles it out without 'umrah, and this supplication and the like in his own language is recommended. (3) His saying: 'then he recites talbiyah' means he performs the talbiyah quietly or loudly, the latter being better. [4] His saying: 'Labbayk Allahumma labbayk' means I stand at Your door time after time, and I answer Your call time after time; the phrase 'Allahumma' is inserted between the emphasized words, and His saying: 'inna' (with kasr, and fath is also permissible). [5] His saying: 'he should not decrease' because it is the transmitted (wording) from the Messenger of Allah. [6] His saying: 'and if he increases' means adding appropriate words to these words, that is permissible, as established from a group of Companions. (1) From Ibn 'Umar: I saw the Messenger of Allah ﷺ pray two rak'ahs at Dhu al-Hulayfah (Sahih Muslim 1:481, Sahih al-Bukhari 2:461). And from Ibn 'Abbas: The Messenger of Allah ﷺ left as a pilgrim, and when he prayed two rak'ahs in his mosque at Dhu al-Hulayfah, he made them obligatory in his assembly, and he began the talbiyah for hajj after finishing his two rak'ahs (al-Mustadrak 1:620, Sunan al-Bayhaqi al-Kabir 5:37, Sunan Abi Dawud 2:150, Musnad Ahmad 1:260, etc.). (2) From Ibn 'Umar: The talbiyah of the Messenger of Allah ﷺ was: 'Labbayk Allahumma labbayk, labbayka la sharika laka labbayk, inna al-hamda wa al-ni'mata laka wa al-mulk, la sharika lak.' And Ibn 'Umar used to add: 'Labbayk labbayk wa sa'dayk wa al-khayr bi yadayk labbayk wa al-raghba' ilayk wa al-'amal' (Sahih Muslim 2:841).
Source pages p. 556

Commentary
This passage details the sunan (recommended acts) after making the intention for ihram. The mufrid bil-hajj (one performing only hajj, not 'umrah) is recommended to pray two rak'ahs of supererogatory prayer (nafl) before wearing the ihram garments, as the Prophet did at Dhu al-Hulayfah. Then he makes a supplication asking Allah to make hajj easy and accept it. Then he recites the talbiyah, the specific wording transmitted from the Prophet: 'Labbayk Allahumma labbayk…'. The commentator explains the meaning of 'labbayk': it signifies repeatedly answering Allah's call. It is recommended not to decrease from this wording, but it is permissible to add other appropriate praises (as Ibn 'Umar did). The talbiyah may be said quietly or loudly; loud is better for men. This passage establishes that the two rak'ahs and the talbiyah are mustahabb (recommended), not obligatory.

Key Terms
شفعاً (shaf'ān): An even number; here meaning two rak'ahs of supererogatory prayer.
المفرد بالحج (al-mufrid bil-hajj): One who performs hajj alone without combining it with 'umrah (i.e., ifrad).
تلبية (talbiyah): The specific formula recited by a pilgrim upon entering ihram, beginning with 'Labbayk Allahumma labbayk'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It is prohibited
B) It is permissible and recommended
C) It is obligatory to add
D) It invalidates the ihram

Key Takeaway
The two-rak'ah prayer and the talbiyah for the mufrid bil-hajj are emphasized sunnahs based on the Prophet's practice; the talbiyah's wording should not be shortened, but additions are allowed.

Talbiyah for the Mufrid: Wording and Permissibility of Additions
Ruling on the wording and recitation of the talbiyah for mufrid al-Hajj

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the transmitted wording of the talbiyah for the mufrid and explain the ruling on reducing or adding to it.

Core Idea
The talbiyah wording is fixed from the Prophet; reduction disallowed but additions permissible.

This lesson addresses the specific wording of the talbiyah recited by one performing Hajj alone (mufrid). The text emphasizes the transmitted text handed down from the Prophet and shows that adding appropriate phrases is allowed, as practiced by Companions. Understanding this balance—preserving the core while permitting expansion—is key to correctly performing this wajib act.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is reciting the talbiyah upon entering ihram. In the Hanafi school, it is wajib (obligatory). The passage focuses on the wording and what changes are allowed.
2
Extract the transmitted wording
The text gives the exact wording: 'Labbayk Allahumma labbayk, labbayka la sharika laka labbayk, inna al-hamda wa al-ni'mata laka wa al-mulk, la sharika lak.' This is the core text that must not be reduced.
The commentary explains each phrase, e.g., 'labbayk' means repeated answering.
3
Analyze the prohibition of reduction
The author says 'la yanqus'—do not reduce it. This is because the wording is directly from the Prophet (sunnah). Omission of any part invalidates the talbiyah.
The dalil is the transmitted practice itself.
4
Analyze the permissibility of addition
The author says 'wa in zad jaza'—if he adds, it is permissible. This is supported by the practice of Ibn Umar and other Companions who added phrases like 'wa sa'dayk'. Additions must be appropriate (alfaz munasibah).
5
Synthesize the ruling
Therefore, the talbiyah has a fixed minimal text that must be recited completely. One may lawfully append extra words of praise or supplication, but may not shorten the transmitted text.
This balances preservation of sunnah with flexibility.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling accompanied by proof. It begins by stating that the person entering ihram for Hajj alone (mufrid) prays two rak'ahs and then makes a specific supplication before reciting the talbiyah. The author stresses that the talbiyah should not be reduced from its transmitted wording, and that adding to it is permissible, as proven by the practice of the Prophet and his Companions. The commentary clarifies each phrase, including the meaning of 'shaf'an' (two rak'ahs), 'mufrid', and the talbiyah wording. The hadith evidence from Bukhari and Muslim is provided. The lesson is straightforward: the talbiyah is a defined act of worship with fixed wording, but additions are allowed.

Key Terms
المفرد بالحج (al-mufrid bi al-hajj): One who assumes ihram for Hajj only from the miqat, without combining it with 'umrah.
التلبية (al-talbiyah): The specific words recited upon entering ihram, beginning with 'Labbayk Allahumma labbayk'.
لبيك (labbayk): Meaning 'I answer Your call again and again,' expressing obedience and readiness.

Key Takeaway
The talbiyah for the mufrid must include the transmitted wording without reduction, but adding appropriate phrases is permissible.

The Three Prohibitions for a Muhrim: Rafath, Fusooq, and Jidal
Definition of the three prohibitions for a muhrim with expanded commentary on rafath.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the three prohibitions for a muhrim (rafath, fusooq, jidal) and explain the nuanced scope of rafath based on the Hanafi understanding.

Core Idea
Rafath includes explicit speech directed at women, not merely any mention of intercourse.

In this lesson, we explore the three major prohibitions that a person in ihram must observe: rafath, fusooq, and jidal. The text from the commentary provides precise definitions, especially clarifying what constitutes rafath through the illustrative story of Ibn Abbas. Understanding these boundaries is essential for the validity and completion of Hajj.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three prohibitions
State the terms rafath, fusooq, and jidal as mentioned in the passage. Note that they are to be avoided by a muhrim.
2
Define rafath according to the text
The passage says rafath includes sexual intercourse, obscene speech, or mentioning intercourse in the presence of women. Understand that the commentary specifies that 'in the presence of women' means directly addressing them, not just mentioning it in general.
Note the nuance from Ibn Abbas: reciting poetry that mentions intercourse but is not directed at women is not rafath.
3
Define fusooq and jidal
Fusooq is defined as sins (plural of fisq), and jidal is disputation. The commentary does not expand on these as much, but they are also prohibited for the muhrim.
4
Analyze the Ibn Abbas episode
Examine the story: Ibn Abbas recited a verse containing a phrase implying intercourse. When questioned, he clarified that rafath is only what is spoken to women. This refines the definition: the prohibition depends on the context and audience.
This demonstrates the Hanafi principle of focusing on the 'illah (cause) – here the incitement to intercourse.
5
Apply the definitions to practical scenarios
Based on the definitions, determine whether certain actions or speech violate the prohibitions. For example, thinking about intercourse is not rafath; only speech directed at women is.
This prepares for practice tasks.

وإذا لبى ناوياً فقد أحرم ، فيتقي الرّفت، والفسوق، والجدال) الرفث : الجماع ، أو الكلام الفاحش " ، أو ذكر الجماع بحضرة النِّساء، فقد رُوِيَ أنّ ابنَ عَبَّاسِ لِما أنشد قوله : ن يَمْشِينَ بِنَا هَمِيْسَا إِنْ يَصْدُقَ الطَّيْرُ نَنِكَ لَمِيسَا قيل له : أترفُتُ وأنت محرم ، فقال ابن عباس : إنما الرَّفتُ ما خُوطِبَ به النِّساء (۱). والضمير في هُنَّ يرجع إلى الإبل، والهميس : صوتُ نَعْل أخفافها .
[١] قوله : فيتقي ; شروع فيما يمتنع منه المحرم.
[٢] قوله : الفسوق ; بالضمّ مصدر ، ويحتمل أن يكون جمع فسق ، كعلم وعلوم، كما يشير إليه تفسيرهم بالمعاصي.

[0]قوله : أنشد ; إنشاد الشعر قرأته لتغني به، ورفع الصوت.
[٦]قوله : وهن يمشين ... الخ ; معناه : أنّ الإبل تمشي بنا بالسرعة واليسر، وهذا يؤذن بوصولنا إلى مقصدنا مع الصحة والعافية، فإن صدق هذا الفال نجا مع اللميس، بوصولنا إليها، فالباء في قوله : «بنا» للتعدية، والهميس على وزن كريم : صوت يخرجُ من نعال أقدام الإبل عند وضعها على الأرض ورفعها حالة مشيها المتوسط المعتدل. وقوله : «إن» شرطيّة، وشرطه يصدق بكسر القاف وضم الدال ، وفاعل الفعل : «الطير» - وهو بالفتح - الفال وجزاؤه قوله : «ننك»، وهو مضارع بصيغة التكلّم مع الغير، من النيك، يقال: ناك المرأة نيكاً - بالفتح - : فهو نائك ; أي جامعها وتلذذ بها، فأصله : ننيك على وزن نبيع، حذفت بالياء للجزم.
(1) ورد هذا الأثر في مصنف ابن أبي شيبة (۳ : ۳۱۰) ، واللفظ له ، وسنن البيهقي الكبرى (٥ : ٦٧) : عن أبي العالية عن ابن عباس الله ، قال تمثل هذا البيت : وهو محرم، قال : وَهُنَّ يَمْشِينَ بِنَا هَمَيْسَا إِنْ يَصْدُقِ الطَّيْرُ نَنِكَ لَمِيْسا قال : فقيل له : تقول هذا وأنت محرم ، فقال : : إنما الفحش ما روجع به النساء وهم محرومون.اهـ.
And when he recites the talbiyah intending ihram, he has entered ihram. So he must avoid al-rafath, al-fusooq, and al-jidal. Al-rafath: sexual intercourse, or obscene speech, or mentioning sexual intercourse in the presence of women. For it is narrated that Ibn Abbas, when he recited the verse: 'And they walk with us with a soft tread, if the bird proves true, we will have intercourse with Lamisa,' it was said to him: 'Do you commit rafath while you are in ihram?' Ibn Abbas said: 'Verily, rafath is what is spoken to women.' The pronoun 'hunna' refers to she-camels, and 'hamis' is the sound of their hooves. [Commentary] His saying: 'yattaqi' begins the explanation of what the muhrim must avoid. His saying: 'al-fusooq' with dammah is a verbal noun, and it may be a plural of fisq, like 'ilm and 'ulum, as indicated by their interpretation as sins. His saying: 'al-fahish' is that which leads to sexual intercourse. His saying: 'bi hadrah' with fatha means directly addressing women, not in their absence, because the incitement to intercourse is the former, not the latter. For this reason Ibn Abbas saw no harm in his poetry that included mention of intercourse. His saying: 'anshada' means reciting poetry with a melody and raising the voice. His saying: 'wa hunna yamshina...' etc. means: The she-camels walk with us quickly and easily, indicating our arrival at our destination with health and well-being; if this augury proves true, we will reach Lamisa. The letter ba in 'bina' is for transitiveness, 'hamis' (like karim) is the sound from the shoes of camels' feet when placed on the ground and lifted during their moderate gait. 'In' is conditional, its condition 'yasduq' with kasr of qaf and damm of dal, its subject 'al-tayr' (with fatha) meaning the omen, its consequence 'nanik', which is the first person plural imperfect of 'nik', meaning to have sexual intercourse and enjoy. Its origin is 'nuniku' on the pattern of 'nabi'u', with the ya omitted for the jussive.
Source pages p. 557

Commentary
This lesson defines the three main prohibitions for a muhrim: rafath (sexual intercourse or explicit speech), fusooq (sinning generally), and jidal (disputation). The commentary focuses on rafath, clarifying that it includes not only the act of intercourse but also any speech that is sexually explicit or alludes to it, especially when addressed to women. The story of Ibn Abbas illustrates the nuance: reciting poetry that includes such references is not considered rafath if it is not directed at women. The commentary also explains the linguistic aspects of the poetic verse.

Key Terms
الرفث (al-rafath): Sexual intercourse, obscene speech, or mentioning sexual intercourse in the presence of women.
الفسوق (al-fusooq): Sins and disobedience generally, as the plural of fisq.
الجدال (al-jidal): Disputation or argumentation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It is only intercourse, not speech.
B) Rafath is what is spoken to women.
C) Rafath includes poetry.
D) Rafath is any obscenity.

Key Takeaway
Rafath for a muhrim is defined as explicit sexual speech or act, and according to the Hanafi view, it must be directed at women to be considered rafath.

The Three Prohibitions of Ihram: Rafath, Fusuq, Jidal
Commentary explaining three prohibitions for the muhrim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three prohibitions upon entering ihram according to the Hanafi school and explain the definition of each, including the nuance of rafath as illustrated by Ibn Abbas.

Core Idea
The muhrim must avoid rafath (obscenity), fusuq (sin), and jidal (argumentation)—each with specific Hanafi definitions.

When a pilgrim enters ihram, certain actions become prohibited. The commentary on al-Wiqayah elaborates on three key prohibitions: rafath, fusuq, and jidal. This lesson unpacks their meanings, including a fascinating incident from Ibn Abbas that clarifies the scope of rafath. Understanding these prohibitions helps ensure the validity and reward of Hajj.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three prohibitions
From the opening line, note the three things the muhrim must avoid: rafath, fusuq, jidal.
These are stated in the Quran (2:197) as general prohibitions during Hajj.
2
Define rafath
The commentary defines rafath as intercourse, obscene speech, or mentioning intercourse in the presence of women. Ibn Abbas's incident shows that the prohibition applies to speech directed at women, not general poetry.
The Hanafi school includes any speech that incites desire.
3
Define fusuq and jidal
Fusuq means sins in general, more severely prohibited during ihram. Jidal means argumentation, especially with companions or the polytheists' calendar manipulation.
The commentary links jidal to the pre-Islamic practice of nasi'.
4
Apply to the Ibn Abbas incident
Ibn Abbas recited a verse implying intercourse but was not culpable because his speech was not addressed to women. This shows the contextual nature of rafath.
The incident is a practical application of the definition.
