The Sunnah Acts Upon Entering Mecca
Sequential sunnah acts upon entering Mecca

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the sunnah acts recommended upon entering Mecca and identify their proofs, distinguishing them from obligatory acts.

Core Idea
Entering the mosque, facing the Black Stone, and raising hands are stressed sunnah, not fard.

Upon arriving in Mecca, a pilgrim is encouraged to perform a sequence of acts that the Prophet (peace be upon him) consistently did. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah details these acts and provides the evidence, clarifying that they are sunnah muakkadah, not obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sequence of acts
List the actions mentioned: entering the mosque first, saying takbir and tahleel when sighting the Kaaba, facing the Black Stone, saying takbir and tahleel again, raising hands like in prayer, and touching the Stone.
These acts are done in order and are separate from the obligatory tawaf.
2
Classify the hukm level
The commentator implies these are sunnah muakkadah (stressed sunnah) by citing the Prophet's practice as proof and not commanding them. Confirm the category using Hanafi fiqh classifications.
Sunnah muakkadah is a meritorious act; omitting it is not sinful but loses reward.
3
Locate the proofs
The passage cites Sahih al-Bukhari, Sahih Muslim, Musnad Ahmad, Sunan Abi Dawud, and al-Tahawi. Note that the acts are transmitted through mutawatir or ahad hadith, giving them strong basis.
The dalil block in the toolbox identifies the hadith as Sunnah_mutawatir with qati thubut.
4
Note the condition
The commentator adds a condition: 'if he is secure about his belongings and companions.' This means the sunnah is conditional on safety; if there is risk, one may delay.
This reflects the Hanafi principle of considering need and harm.
5
Understand the broader practice
Ibrahim al-Nakha'i lists seven places where hands are raised, situating this act within a larger set of sunnah practices in worship.
This shows internal coherence in Hanafi fiqh: the same action is performed in several contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا دَخَلَ مكة بدأ بالمسجد، وحين رأى البيت كبرَ وهلل ، ثُمَّ استقبلَ الْحَجَرَ الأسود، وكبرَ وهَلَّل، ويرفع يديه كالصلاة واستلمه. [١] قوله : بدأ بالمسجد ؛ أي دخل المسجد الحرام ،ابتداء، هو المروي عن رسول الله ، أخرجه الشيخان، وهذا إذا أمن على أمتعته) ورفقته. [٢]قوله : استقبل ؛ أي قام مواجهاً للحجرِ الأسود المركوز في ركن من أركان الكعبة. [٣] قوله : كبر ؛ أي قال الله أكبر ولا إله إلا الله، هو المروي عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في «مسند أحمد»، و«صحيح البخاري»، والابتداء بالحجرِ أيضاً ثبت عنه في «سنن أبي داود وغيره. [٤] قوله : ويرفع يديه كالصلاة ؛ أي كما يرفعُ في الصلاة حذو المنكبين أو الأذنين ، قال إبراهيم النخعي الله : ترفع الأيدي في سبعة مواطن: في افتتاح الصلاة، وفي التكبير للقنوت في الوتر، وفي العيدين وعند استلام الحجر، وعلى الصفا والمروة، وبجمع، وعرفات، وعند المقامين، وعند الحجرتين ) ، أخرجه الطحاوي في «شرح معاني الآثار».
And when he enters Mecca, he begins with the mosque, and when he sees the House (Kaaba), he says the Takbir and Tahleel, then he faces the Black Stone, and says the Takbir and Tahleel, and he raises his hands like in prayer, and touches it. [1] His statement: 'he begins with the mosque' means he enters the Sacred Mosque initially; this is narrated from the Messenger of Allah, recorded by al-Bukhari and Muslim, and this is when he is secure about his belongings and companions. [2] His statement: 'he faces' means he stands facing the Black Stone fixed in one of the corners of the Kaaba. [3] His statement: 'he says Takbir' means he says 'Allahu Akbar' and 'La ilaha illallah'; this is narrated from the Messenger of Allah in Musnad Ahmad and Sahih al-Bukhari, and starting with the Stone is also proven from him in Sunan Abi Dawud and others. [4] His statement: 'and he raises his hands like in prayer' means as he raises in prayer to the level of the shoulders or ears. Ibrahim al-Nakha'i said: 'The hands are raised in seven places: at the beginning of prayer, in the Takbir for Qunut in Witr, on the two Eids, when touching the Stone, on Safa and Marwah, at Muzdalifah and Arafat, and at the two Stations and the two Stones.' Recorded by al-Tahawi in Sharh Ma'ani al-Athar.
Source pages p. 563

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah details the recommended actions for a pilgrim upon entering Mecca. Upon arrival, the pilgrim should proceed directly to the Sacred Mosque, if safe to do so, following the practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) as recorded by al-Bukhari and Muslim. When the Kaaba comes into sight, it is recommended to say 'Allahu Akbar' and 'La ilaha illallah' to express reverence. Then, the pilgrim should face the Black Stone directly, standing in front of it, and again say the Takbir and Tahleel. Finally, the pilgrim raises his hands as in prayer (to the shoulders or ears) and touches the Stone. The commentator cites Ibrahim al-Nakha'i listing seven occasions where raising hands is prescribed: in the opening takbir of prayer, in qunut of witr, in Eid prayers, upon touching the Black Stone, on Safa and Marwah, at Muzdalifah and Arafat, and at the Stations and the Two Stones. This shows the Hanafi school's attention to specific acts of worship derived from the Prophet's practice.

Key Terms
الحجر الأسود (al-ḥajar al-aswad): The Black Stone, set in the eastern corner of the Kaaba, which is kissed or touched during tawaf.
استلمه (istalamahu): To touch or kiss the Black Stone as a form of veneration.
رفع اليدين (rafʿ al-yadayn): Raising the hands to the shoulders or ears, as done in prayer opening takbir, also at other occasions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Five places
C) Seven places
D) Nine places

Key Takeaway
The sunnah upon entering Mecca includes entering the mosque, saying takbir and tahleel, facing the Black Stone, raising hands, and touching it, provided it is safe.

Sunnah Acts upon Entering Makkah: Kaaba, Black Stone, and Tawaf al-Qudum
Explanation of sunnah acts upon entering Makkah for Hajj/Umrah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the sequence of sunnah acts upon entering Makkah, identify the Hadith evidence for each step, and explain why the Hanafi school considers Tawaf al-Qudum sunnah rather than wajib.

Core Idea
Interacting with the Black Stone is sunnah, not obligatory; if crowded, face it and glorify Allah.

When a pilgrim enters Makkah, specific actions are recommended to honor the Kaaba and the Black Stone. This lesson unpacks the text from Al-Wiqayah and its commentary, clarifying the order of acts and the prophetic guidance that prioritizes gentleness over zeal. We will also see how the Hanafi school derives the sunnah status of Tawaf al-Qudum from the evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sequence of acts
List the actions in order: enter the mosque, upon seeing the Kaaba say Takbir and Tahlil, then face the Black Stone, say Takbir and Tahlil again, raise hands, kiss the Stone if possible (or touch with hand/object, then kiss), then perform Tawaf al-Qudum for the afaqi.
The matn gives the baseline; the commentary expands each phrase.
2
Locate the Hadith proofs
Two Hadiths are cited: (1) The Prophet's instruction to Umar not to crowd but to kiss if there is a gap, otherwise face and recite. (2) The Prophet touching the Stone with a stick. These are ahad Hadiths, giving zanni evidence.
The first Hadith is from Ahmad and Abu Ya'la; the second from Bukhari.
3
Trace the legal implication (wajh al-dalalah)
The command to face and recite when unable to kiss shows that kissing is not obligatory—an alternative exists. The Hanafi principle is that a command in such a context indicates recommendation (nadb) not obligation (wujub).
This is a key Hanafi usul point: the alternative indicates the original is not binding.
4
Note the ikhtilaf and its thamarah
Some Shafi'i scholars consider Tawaf al-Qudum as wajib for the afaqi. The Hanafi position is sunnah. The practical difference: omitting a wajib requires dam (penalty); omitting a sunnah does not.
The text explicitly says 'Sunnah for the afaqi' confirming the Hanafi view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحج باب الإحرام
وإذا دَخَلَ مكة بدأ بالمسجد، وحين رأى البيت كبرَ وهلل ، ثُمَّ استقبلَ الْحَجَرَ الأسود، وكبرَ وهَلَّل، ويرفع يديه كالصلاة واستلمه إن قَدِرَ غير مؤذ ، وإلا يُمِس شيئاً في يده ، ثُمَّ قَبلَه ، وإِن عَجَزَ عنهما استقبله ، وكبر وهلل ، وحَمِدَ الله تعالى، وصلّى على النبي عليه السلام ، وطاف طواف القدوم ، وسُن للآفاقي
[قوله : إن قدر ... الخ] لقول رسول الله : «إنك رجلُ أيد تؤذي الضعيف، فلا تزاحم الناس على الحجر، لكن إن وجدت فرجة فاستلمه، وإلا فاستقبله ، وهلل وكبر » ، أخرجه أحمد وأبو يعلي وغيرهما.
[قوله : يمس] بضم الياء وكسر الميم مضارع من الامساس : أي إن لم يمكنه أن يقبله بفمه أو يمسه بيده ، يمسه شيئاً في يده كالعصا ونحوه، وقد ثبت أنّ رسول الله استلمه بالعصا، أخرجه البخاري وغيره.
[قوله : استقبله] أي يقوم مقابلاً له بوجهه ويشير بباطن كفيه نحو الحجر، ويجعل ظاهرهما نحو وجهه، رافعاً يديه حذاء أذنيه أو منكبيه، ويكبر ويهلل ويحمد الله ويُصلي على النبي.
[قوله : وسن] بصيغة المجهول يعني أنّ طواف القدوم الذي يكون عند قدومه سنة لمن يدخل مكة من خارجها لا للمكي، وهذا الطواف للمفرد بالحج ، وأما المتمتع
The Book of Hajj, Chapter of Ihram. When he enters Makkah, he begins at the mosque, and when he sees the House (Kaaba), he says Takbir and Tahlil, then he faces the Black Stone, says Takbir and Tahlil, raises his hands as in prayer, and kisses it if he is able without harming (someone), otherwise he touches something in his hand, then kisses it, and if he is unable to do both, he faces it, says Takbir and Tahlil, praises Allah, sends blessings on the Prophet (peace be upon him), and performs Tawaf al-Qudum. It is Sunnah for the one coming from outside (Makkah). [His saying: 'if he is able... etc.'] For the saying of the Messenger of Allah: 'O Umar, you are a strong man who harms the weak, so do not crowd people at the Stone. But if you find a gap, then kiss it, otherwise face it and say Tahlil and Takbir.' Narrated by Ahmad, Abu Ya'la, and others. [His saying: 'touches'] With damma on the ya and kasr on the mim, present tense from amsas: meaning if he cannot kiss it with his mouth or touch it with his hand, he touches it with something in his hand like a stick or similar. It is established that the Messenger of Allah touched it with a stick, narrated by Bukhari and others. [His saying: 'faces it'] Meaning he stands facing it with his face and gestures with the inner side of his palms towards the Stone, making their outer side towards his face, raising his hands level with his ears or shoulders, and says Takbir, Tahlil, praises Allah, and sends blessings on the Prophet. [His saying: 'and it is Sunnah'] In the passive voice means that Tawaf al-Qudum, which occurs at his arrival, is Sunnah for the one who enters Makkah from outside, not for the Makkan. This Tawaf is for the one performing Hajj ifrad (single Hajj). As for the mutamatti'...
Source pages p. 563

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the sunnah acts upon entering Makkah for Hajj or Umrah. The author begins by stating that when one enters Makkah, he should first go to the Masjid al-Haram. Upon setting eyes on the Kaaba, he should recite the Takbir (Allahu Akbar) and Tahlil (La ilaha illallah). Then he should face the Black Stone (al-Hajar al-Aswad) and again recite Takbir and Tahlil, raising his hands as he does in prayer (i.e., to the level of his shoulders or ears). He should then kiss the Stone if he is able to do so without harming other Muslims or causing a disturbance. If he cannot kiss it directly due to crowds, he may touch it with his hand or something he is holding (like a stick) and then kiss that object. If even that is not possible, he should simply face the Stone, raise his hands, and recite the praises. After this, he performs Tawaf al-Qudum (the arrival circumambulation). This Tawaf is a sunnah for the afaqi (one who comes from outside Makkah), not for the resident of Makkah. The author supports these actions with hadith: the Prophet commanded Umar not to crowd the weak but to kiss the Stone if there is a gap, otherwise to face it and recite. Another hadith shows the Prophet himself touched the Stone with a stick. The commentator elaborates on each phrase: 'yamussu' means to touch with something, 'istaqbala' means to face and gesture with palms. The ruling emphasizes respect for the sacred rites and consideration for other worshippers.

Key Terms
استلام (istilām): The act of touching or kissing the Black Stone as a form of reverence.
طواف القدوم (ṭawāf al-qudūm): The circumambulation performed upon first arriving in Makkah by one coming from outside.
آفاقي (āfāqī): A person who enters Makkah from outside the miqat boundaries, i.e., a non-resident of Makkah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Only the afaqi (one coming from outside Makkah)
D) Both the afaqi and the Makki if they are performing Umrah

Key Takeaway
Upon entering Makkah, the pilgrim performs recommended acts of reverence for the Kaaba and Black Stone, all of which are sunnah muakkadah, with graded options to avoid harming others.

6 min
Sunan of Tawaf: Starting Point, Idtiba', and the Hatim
Rulings on recommended acts during circumambulation with hadith proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three sunan of tawaf mentioned in the Hanafi text and apply the ruling that tawaf inside al-Hatim is invalid.

Core Idea
Six cubits of al-Hatim are part of the Kaaba; tawaf must encompass the entire House.

This lesson examines three recommended acts (sunan) of tawaf as transmitted in the Hanafi school. These acts are not obligatory but following them completes the sunna of the Prophet. The most critical point concerns the semi-circular wall known as al-Hatim: because part of it belongs to the Kaaba, circumambulating inside it invalidates the tawaf. We will trace the proofs and understand the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three sunan of tawaf
From the text, extract the three recommended acts: (1) starting the tawaf from the Black Stone and proceeding towards the door, (2) performing idtiba' (wearing the garment under the right armpit and over the left shoulder), and (3) circumambulating outside al-Hatim (the semi-circular wall).
The starting point is the Black Stone, not the door. The area between the Stone and the door is called al-multazam.
2
Locate the dalil for each sunna
For the starting point and idtiba', the text implies the practice of the Prophet (though no explicit hadith is quoted). For the Hatim ruling, the text quotes the hadith from Sahih Muslim: 'Six cubits of it are from the House.' The Quranic verse 'let them circumambulate the Ancient House' (22:29) is also cited.
The proofs are ahad (single-transmitter) hadith, thus zanni in transmission, but accepted for sunna rulings.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah for the Hatim
The hadith directly states that part of the Hatim is from the Kaaba. Therefore, circumambulating inside the Hatim (between the Kaaba and the Hatim wall) means one has not encompassed the entire Kaaba, violating the Quranic command to circumambulate the 'House.' Hence, such a tawaf is invalid.
The Hanafi position is that tawaf outside the entire perimeter of the Kaaba, including the Hatim, is obligatory; tawaf inside is not valid.
4
Apply the ruling to a scenario
If a pilgrim circumambulates through the gap between the Kaaba and the Hatim wall (i.e., inside the Hatim), the tawaf does not count and must be repeated. The pilgrim must walk outside the Hatim's wall.
This is a common mistake; many pilgrims are unaware that the Hatim is considered part of the Kaaba.
5
Note additional rulings: the qarin
The text adds that a qarin (one combining Hajj and Umrah) is sufficed by the tawaf of Umrah; he may optionally perform an extra tawaf al-qudum on arrival.
This is a subsidiary point; the main lesson is on the sunan and the Hatim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسن للآفاقي، وأخذ عن يمينه ، فيبتدئ مما يلي الباب ، جاعلاً رداءه تحت إبطه اليمين ملقياً طرفه على كتفه اليُسرى. وسن للآفاقي، وأخذ عن يمينه ، فيبتدى " مما يلي الباب)، الضمير في يمينه يرجعُ الطائف، فالطائف المستقبلُ للحجر يكونُ يمينه إلى جانب الباب، فيبتدئ من الحجر ذاهباً إلى هذا الجانب، وهو الملتزم أي ما بين الحجر إلى الباب، جاعلاً رداءه تحت إبطه اليمين ملقياً طرفه على كتفه اليُسرى. وفي «المختصر» : قلت : مضطبعا، ومعنى الاضطباع هذا. والقارنُ فيكفيهما طواف العمرة، ويجوز للقارن أن يطوف للقدوم بعد طواف العمرة. كذا في «اللباب». (ووراء الحطيم سبعة أشواط ) : الخَطِيمُ مشتق من الحطم ، وهو الكسر، وهو موضع فيه الميزاب، سمي بهذا لأنه حُطِمَ من البيت : أي كسر، روي عن عائشة رضي الله عنها أنَّها نَذَرت إن فَتَحَ اللهُ تعالى مكة على رسوله أن تصلي في البيتِ ركعتين، فلما فتحت مكة أَخَذَ رسولُ الله بيدها وأدخلَها الحَطيم. قوله : وراء الحطيم؛ وهو الموضع الذي أحيط بجدار كنصف دائرة الخارج من الكعبة في جانب ميزاب الرحمة، وقد ثبت في صحيح مسلم مرفوعا : «إن ستة أذرع منه من البيت». ويشهده قوله تعالى: وَلْيَطُوفُوا بِالْبَيْتِ الْعَتِيقِ، فلو طاف داخلاً من الفرجة التي بين الكعبة وجدار الحطيم لم يجز طوافه.
It is sunna for the one coming from outside to start from his right, beginning from what is next to the door, placing his garment under his right armpit, throwing its end over his left shoulder. It is sunna for the one coming from outside to start from his right, beginning from what is next to the door. The pronoun 'his right' refers to the circumambulator. When the circumambulator faces the Black Stone, his right is towards the door. So he starts from the Black Stone going towards this side, which is al-multazam, i.e., between the Stone and the door, placing his garment under his right armpit etc. In al-Mukhtasar: I said: idtiba', and the meaning of idtiba' is this. The qarin (one performing combined Hajj and Umrah) is sufficed by the tawaf of Umrah, and it is permissible for the qarin to perform tawaf al-qudum after tawaf al-umrah. So in al-Lubab. (And behind al-Hatim seven rounds): Al-Hatim is derived from hatm meaning breaking; it is the place where the rainwater spout is; it is called that because it was broken off from the House. It is narrated from Aisha that she vowed to pray two rak'ahs in the Kaaba if Allah conquered Mecca for His Messenger; when Mecca was conquered, the Prophet took her hand and entered her into al-Hatim. The statement 'behind al-Hatim' refers to the place that is surrounded by a wall like a semicircle outside the Kaaba on the side of the spout of mercy. It is established in Sahih Muslim marfu'an: 'Six cubits of it are from the House.' And His statement 'let them circumambulate the Ancient House' supports this. So if one circumambulates inside the gap between the Kaaba and the wall of al-Hatim, his tawaf is not valid.
Source pages p. 565

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, explaining the sunan (recommended acts) of tawaf. The author first states the sunna acts: starting the tawaf from the right side, beginning near the door (i.e., from the Black Stone going towards the door), and performing idtiba' (wearing the garment under the right armpit and over the left shoulder). The commentary clarifies that the circumambulator should start at the Black Stone and proceed towards the door, and the area between the Black Stone and the door is called al-multazam, where it is recommended to press one's body after tawaf. Idtiba' is specifically recommended during the entire tawaf and is abandoned when the tawaf is done. For a person performing Hajj qiran (combining Umrah and Hajj), the tawaf of Umrah suffices, and he may perform an additional tawaf al-qudum (arrival tawaf) if he wishes. The second section discusses the area of al-Hatim (the semi-circular wall adjacent to the Kaaba). It is part of the Kaaba; six cubits of it originally belonged to the Kaaba. Therefore, tawaf must be performed outside the Hatim, not inside the space between the Kaaba and the Hatim wall. The proof is a hadith from Sahih Muslim stating that six cubits of the Hatim are from the Kaaba, and the Quranic verse 'let them circumambulate the Ancient House'. If someone circumambulates inside the Hatim (the gap), that tawaf is invalid because it does not encompass the entire Kaaba.

Key Terms
الاضطباع (idtiba'): Wearing the upper garment (rida') under the right armpit and over the left shoulder during tawaf.
الملتزم (al-multazam): The area between the Black Stone and the door of the Kaaba, where it is recommended to press one's chest and hands after completing tawaf.
الحطيم (al-Hatim): The semi-circular wall opposite the spout of the Kaaba; part of it (six cubits) is originally from the Kaaba.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) It is permissible with a valid excuse.

Key Takeaway
The Hatim is considered part of the Kaaba; tawaf inside it is invalid because it does not encompass the entire House.

Scope of Raml in Tawaf: Black Stone to Black Stone
Clarifies that raml is from Black Stone to Black Stone

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the sunnah nature of raml and identify its correct scope (from Black Stone to Black Stone) in the first three circuits of tawaf.

Core Idea
Raml is performed from the Black Stone to the Black Stone in the first three circuits – the settled practice of the Prophet and Companions.

This passage clarifies the precise scope of raml (brisk walking with shoulder movements) in tawaf. While some early practices limited raml to the stretch between the Black Stone and the Yemeni corner, the established sunnah — as recorded in the Hanafi tradition — is to perform raml throughout the entire first three circuits, from the Black Stone back to the Black Stone. Understanding this detail ensures that the ritual is performed in complete accordance with the Prophetic practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Raml is brisk walking with short steps and shoulder movements, performed by men in tawaf. Its ruling is sunnah muakkadah (emphasized sunnah). Determine why it is sunnah and not wajib.
Raml is specific to men and to the first three circuits of the first tawaf of Hajj and 'Umrah.
2
Understand the scope of raml
The passage states that raml is performed 'from al-Hajar to al-Hajar' i.e., from the Black Stone to the Black Stone, covering the entire circuit. Note the earlier practice: some would raml only to the Yemeni corner and then walk normally.
The term 'al-Hajar' in both instances refers to the Black Stone (al-Hajar al-Aswad).
3
Trace the dalil (evidence)
The proof for this scope is the continuous practice (amal) of the Prophet ﷺ and his Companions. The text indicates that initially they used to raml only to the Yemeni corner, but later the complete practice became from Black Stone to Black Stone.
This shows how the sunnah was established and settled over time.
4
Apply the ruling practically
In practice, a male pilgrim should perform raml from the moment he passes the Black Stone until he returns to it, for each of the first three circuits. For the remaining four circuits, he walks normally.
Raml is not performed in tawaf that is not the first (e.g., tawaf al-ifadah or tawaf al-wada).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورَمَلَ في الثلاثة الأول فقط من الحجر إلى الحجر. [٣] قوله : من الحجر؛ أي في جميع الدورة من الحجر الأسود إليه. [٤]قوله : الحجر؛ الأول بالفتح؛ أي بالحجر الأسود، والثاني أيضاً بمعنى الحجر الأسود. [١] قوله : إلى الحجر؛ هذا هو الذي استقر عليه عملُ رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وأصحابه، وقد كانوا في الابتداء يرملون من الحجر الأسود إلى الركن اليماني، ويمشون على

[4] His statement: 'al-Hajar' the first is with fatha (meaning the Black Stone), and the second also means the Black Stone. [1] His statement: 'to al-Hajar' this is what the practice of the Messenger of God ﷺ and his companions settled upon; initially they would raml from the Black Stone to the Yemeni corner, and walk on...

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn that states that raml (brisk walking with shoulder movements) is performed in the first three circuits of tawaf, from the Black Stone to the Black Stone. The commentator explains that 'from al-Hajar' means the entire circuit from the Black Stone to itself—i.e., the starting and ending point is the Black Stone. The first 'al-Hajar' refers to the Black Stone (al-Hajar al-Aswad), and the second similarly refers to the Black Stone. The commentator further notes that the established practice of the Prophet ﷺ and his Companions was to perform raml from the Black Stone to the Black Stone. Initially, they would raml from the Black Stone only to the Yemeni corner and then walk normally for the rest, but later the complete practice became from Black Stone to Black Stone. The text cuts off before completing the explanation about walking after the Yemeni corner.

Key Terms
الرمل (ar-raml): Quick walking with short steps and shoulder movements; a sunnah act in the first three circuits of tawaf for men.
الحجر الأسود (al-hajar al-aswad): The Black Stone located in the eastern corner of the Kaaba; the starting and ending point for raml in tawaf.

The Ruling on Istilam: Touching vs Kissing the Yemeni Corner
Ruling on istilam with intra-Hanafi difference

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on istilam of the Black Stone and Yemeni Corner and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute on kissing the Yemeni Corner.

Core Idea
The Yemeni Corner is touched, not kissed — the relied-upon Hanafi view.

This passage from Umda al-Ri'aya explains the sunnah actions during tawaf regarding the Black Stone and the Yemeni Corner. It clarifies that while the Black Stone is kissed, the Yemeni Corner is only touched. However, Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani recommends kissing it, showing nuance within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actions prescribed
Read the passage and note what actions are performed at the Black Stone and the Yemeni Corner. The matn says: whenever passing the Black Stone, do what was mentioned (kiss/touch); and touch the Yemeni Corner.
The Yemeni Corner is touched (yastalamu) but not kissed (la taqbilahu).
2
Understand 'istilam'
Istilam means touching with the hand as a gesture of respect. For the Yemeni Corner, it is done with both palms or the right hand.
Not kissing.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi disagreement
The author states 'la taqbilahu' (don't kiss it) as the majority view, but also reports from Muhammad al-Shaybani that he recommends kissing it.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.
4
Examine the hadith evidence
The Prophet used to touch the Yemeni Corner and the Black Stone, but not the other two corners. The hadith does not mention kissing the Yemeni Corner, supporting the majority view.
The Black Stone is unique; it is from Paradise, so kissing it is specific.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position for practice is to touch the Yemeni Corner without kissing. The author presents Muhammad's view as a minority, not the operative ruling.
Both views are valid, but the dominant practice is not to kiss.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكلما مر بالحجر فعل ما ذكر، ويستلم الركن اليماني، وهو حسن.
[٢] قوله: وكلما مر... الخ؛ أي يفعل ما ذكر من التقبيل والاستلام وغير ذلك بالحجر الأسود في كل دورة.
[٣] قوله: ويستلم الركن اليماني؛ هو طرف الكعبة الذي يلي الحجر الأسود من جانب يسار الطائف إذا قام مستقبل الحجر، واستلامه لمسه بيده بكفيه أو بيمينه، لا تقبيله، وعن محمد يستحب تقبيله.
وقد ثبت في موطأ مالك، والصحيحين وغيرهما: أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم كان يستلم الركن اليماني والحجر الأسود لا الركنين الباقيين من أركان الكعبة، يعرف أحدهما بالركن العراقي والثاني بالشامي.
And whenever he passes by the Black Stone, he does what was mentioned, and he touches (yastalamu) the Yemeni Corner, and it is good. [2] His saying: 'and whenever he passes... etc.': i.e., he does what was mentioned of kissing, touching, etc., at the Black Stone in every circuit. [3] His saying: 'and he touches the Yemeni Corner': It is the side of the Kaaba that is next to the Black Stone on the left side of the circumambulator when he stands facing the Black Stone. Its istilam is to touch it with his hand, with both palms or with his right hand, not kissing it. And from Muhammad (al-Shaybani) it is recommended to kiss it. And it is established in the Muwatta' of Malik, the two Sahihs, and others that the Prophet (peace be upon him) used to touch (yastalamu) the Yemeni Corner and the Black Stone, not the other two corners of the Kaaba, one known as the Iraqi Corner and the second as the Syrian Corner.
Source pages p. 572

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning istilam during tawaf, specifically regarding the Black Stone and the Yemeni Corner. The author begins by restating the matn: whenever the pilgrim passes the Black Stone (al-hajar) in each circuit, he does what was mentioned earlier (kissing or touching). And he touches (yastalamu) the Yemeni Corner, and this is good. The commentary explains that 'what was mentioned' includes both kissing and touching of the Black Stone. The Yemeni Corner is then defined: it is the corner of the Kaaba adjacent to the Black Stone, on the left side of the person circumambulating when facing the Black Stone. Its istilam is to touch it with the hands (both palms or just the right hand), but not to kiss it. However, there is a narration from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani that recommends kissing it, indicating an intra-Hanafi dispute. The passage then provides evidence from the Muwatta' of Malik and the Sahihayn that the Prophet used to touch the Yemeni Corner and the Black Stone, but not the other two corners (Iraqi and Syrian). This establishes the sunnah of istilam for these two corners only.

Key Terms
الاستلام (al-istilam): Touching the Black Stone or the Yemeni Corner with the hand as a gesture of respect during tawaf.
الركن اليماني (al-rukn al-yamani): The corner of the Kaaba facing Yemen, adjacent to the Black Stone.
التقبيل (al-taqbil): Kissing the Black Stone or the Yemeni Corner; the latter is disputed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Yemeni Corner is to be touched, not kissed, according to the relied-upon Hanafi view; the minority view of Muhammad allows kissing, but the majority position is preferred.

The Obligation of the Two Rak'ahs After Tawaf
Ruling on concluding tawaf and the obligatory prayer after it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the two rak'ahs after tawaf, cite the proof from hadith, and explain why it is wajib rather than sunnah.

Core Idea
The two rak'ahs after tawaf are wajib—derived from the Prophet's constant practice and the Quranic command to take Maqam Ibrahim as a place of prayer.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah explains the sequence after completing tawaf: concluding with istilam, praying two rak'ahs, then proceeding to Safa. It clarifies that the two rak'ahs are wajib (obligatory) according to the Hanafi school, supported by the Prophet's action and the verse about Maqam Ibrahim. We will examine the proof and the rationale for the wajib status.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The act is praying two rak'ahs after completing tawaf. The ruling given is 'wajib' (obligatory) after every full circuit (usbu').
Note: The prayer is wajib, not fard, because the evidence is zanni al-thubut.
2
Locate the dalil
The passage cites two proofs: (1) The Prophet's practice recorded in Sahihayn and Sunan that he always prayed two rak'ahs after tawaf. (2) The verse {وَاتَّخِذُوا مِن مَّقَامِ إِبْرَاهِيمَ مُصَلًّى} (Take the Station of Abraham as a place of prayer).
The hadith is ahad, hence zanni al-thubut; the verse is a command but not specifically tied to tawaf.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the dalil prove wujub? The Hanafi principle: The continuous action of the Prophet (fi'l al-nabi) indicates obligation unless there is evidence it is recommended. Here, the Prophet never omitted it, so it becomes wajib. The verse reinforces that the Maqam is a place for prayer, and the Prophet prayed there.
This is a key Hanafi usul point: fi'l al-nabi yufidu al-wujub idha lam yadull 'ala al-nadb.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam al-Shafi'i considers these two rak'ahs as sunnah, not wajib. Their reasoning: the Prophet's act is for recommendation unless there is a command. The verse is not directly commanding the prayer after tawaf.
The practical difference: if missed, a Hanafi must compensate (dam); a Shafi'i does not.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is the mufta bihi (operative ruling) in the school. It is based on the stronger evidence (continuous practice) and the principle that the Prophet's act is for wujub. Al-Marghinani in Al-Hidayah affirms this.
The tarjih is supported by the fact that the Prophet never abandoned this practice, and it is linked to a Quranic command in context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخَتَمَ الطَّواف باستلام الحجر، ثُمَّ صلّى شفعاً يجب بعد كل أسبوع عند المقام أو غيره من المسجد، ثم عاد واستلم الحجر، وخرج فصعد الصفا، واستقبل البيت.
ثم قال الشارح: [١] قوله: باستلام الحجر؛ أي بعدما يفرغ من الأشواط السبعة يستلمه أيضاً، كالبداية لتوافق الخاتمة بالبداية.
[٢] قوله: يجب... الخ؛ الجملة صفة للشفع: أي يصلّي بعد الفراغ من الطواف ركعتين، وهو واجب بعد كل أسبوع - وهو بالضم - عبارة عن الطواف الواحد: أي مجموع الدورات السبع، وهو مخيّر في هذا الأداء في أي موضع كان، وليس أن يؤديهما عند مقام إبراهيم عليه السلام، وهو الحجر الذي عليه أثر قدمه الموضوع بقرب الكعبة. وقد ثبت في الصحيحين والسنن: «أنّ رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم بعدما طاف صلى ركعتين»، وفي بعض الروايات: أنه قرأ: {وَاتَّخِذُوا مِن مَّقَامِ إِبْرَاهِيمَ مُصَلًّى}، فاستفيد منه الوجوب.
[٣] قوله: وخرج... الخ؛ يعني بعدما فرغ من الطواف يخرج من المسجد الحرام إلى جبل الصفا، وهو متصل بجبل أبي قبيس ويصعد عليه، ويستقبل الكعبة ويقول: الله أكبر ولا إله إلا الله، ويصلي على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، هكذا ثبت في «صحيح مسلم» وغيره عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وأصحابه.
[٤] قوله: الصفا؛ الابتداء به الحديث: «ابدؤوا بما بدأ الله به»، يعني في قوله تعالى: {إِنَّ الصَّفَا وَالْمَرْوَةَ مِن شَعَائِرِ اللَّهِ}.
He concluded the tawaf by touching the Black Stone, then he prayed two rak'ahs which are obligatory after every circuit at the Station [of Ibrahim] or elsewhere in the mosque, then he returned and touched the Black Stone, and went out and ascended Safa, and faced the Kaaba. Then the commentator said: [1] His saying: 'by touching the Black Stone'; meaning after he completes the seven circuits, he also touches it, like the beginning, so that the end matches the beginning. [2] His saying: 'obligatory...etc.'; the clause is an adjective for 'two rak'ahs': i.e., he prays two rak'ahs after finishing tawaf, and it is obligatory after every single circuit (usbu')—which with a damma refers to one tawaf: i.e., the total of seven circuits—and he has the choice in its performance wherever he is, and it is not that he must perform them at the Station of Ibrahim (peace be upon him), which is the stone with the imprint of his foot placed near the Kaaba. It has been established in the Sahihayn and Sunan: 'that the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, after performing tawaf prayed two rak'ahs', and in some narrations: that he recited: {And take the Station of Abraham as a place of prayer}, so obligation is derived from it. [3] His saying: 'and went out...etc.'; meaning after he finishes tawaf, he exits the Sacred Mosque to the mount of Safa, which is connected to the mount of Abu Qubays, and ascends it, and faces the Kaaba, and says: Allahu Akbar, La ilaha illa Allah, and sends blessings upon the Prophet, peace be upon him; thus it is established in Sahih Muslim and others from the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, and his companions. [4] His saying: 'Safa'; starting with it [is based on] the hadith: 'Start with what Allah started with', meaning in His saying: {Indeed, Safa and Marwah are among the symbols of Allah}.
Source pages p. 573

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It states the actions after completing tawaf: first, one touches the Black Stone again to conclude the tawaf, matching the beginning. Then one must pray two rak'ahs after every seven-circuit tawaf. This obligation is proven by the Prophet's ﷺ practice, as recorded in authentic hadiths, and by the verse instructing to take the Maqam Ibrahim as a prayer place. The two rak'ahs may be performed anywhere in the mosque, not necessarily right at the Maqam. After the prayer, one again touches the Black Stone, then proceeds to the hill of Safa, faces the Kaaba, and makes takbir and tahmid. The order of starting with Safa follows the Quranic order, as the Prophet ﷺ said, 'Start with what Allah started with.'

Key Terms
استلام الحجر (istilām al-ḥajar): Touching or kissing the Black Stone during tawaf; here it refers to the concluding touch after completing seven circuits.
شفع (shaf‘): Two rak'ahs of prayer performed after tawaf; the text states they are wajib.
أسبوع (usbū‘): A single complete tawaf consisting of seven circuits around the Kaaba.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on the two rak'ahs after tawaf?
A) Sunnah
B) Wajib
C) Fard
D) Mustahabb

Key Takeaway
The two rak'ahs after tawaf are wajib in the Hanafi school, based on the Prophet's constant practice and the Quranic command, with the practical consequence that missing them requires a dam.

Actions of Sa'y and the Definition of a Circuit
Description of sa'y actions and dispute over circuit definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the actions of sa'y according to the Hanafi school and state the relied-upon definition of a circuit (shaut), distinguishing it from the minority view.

Core Idea
Sa'y consists of seven crossings between Safa and Marwah; a circuit is one crossing, not a round trip.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains the detailed actions of sa'y after tawaf. It also introduces an important intra-Hanafi dispute about the number of circuits: does a shaut mean one leg or a round trip? Understanding this clarifies the correct performance of this wajib rite.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that sa'y is a wajib act in Hajj and Umrah according to the Hanafi school. Its performance is detailed in the text.
2
Outline the standard procedure
The pilgrim starts at Safa, makes takbir and supplication, then walks to Marwah, running between the two green markers. At Marwah, he repeats the supplication. This is done seven times, starting at Safa and ending at Marwah.
The two green markers are actually one green and one yellow; the term 'green' is used by predominance.
3
Define a circuit (shaut) per the majority
In the standard Hanafi view, one circuit is a single crossing from one hill to the other. Thus, seven circuits mean seven back-and-forths, starting at Safa and ending at Marwah.
4
Contrast with al-Tahawi's alternative definition
Al-Tahawi reports that a round trip from Safa to Marwah and back to Safa constitutes one circuit, resulting in fourteen crossings and ending at Safa.
This view is considered a mistake by many Hanafi jurists.
5
Understand the tarjih
The majority view (seven circuits, each one crossing, ending at Marwah) is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position. The text dismisses al-Tahawi's view as incorrect.
This dispute is intra-Hanafi; the Shafi'i school considers sa'y a rukn, while Hanafis consider it wajib.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكبر وهلل، وصلى على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، ورفع يديه ودعا بما شاء، ثم مشى نحو المروة ساعياً بين الميلين الأخضرين، وصعد عليها، وفعل ما فعله على الصفا، يفعل هكذا سبعاً، يبدأ بالصفا، ويختم بالمروة.
ثم قال الشارح: [١] قوله: ورفع يديه؛ أي للدعاء، فإنه يستحب في كل دعاء.
[٢] قوله: ثم مشى؛ أي ينزل من الصفا ذاهباً إلى جانب المروة – وهو بالفتح: جبل -، ويمشي على هيئته، ويسعى بين الميلين الأخضرين في بطن الوادي إلى أن يرتفع منه، وهما العلامتان المركوزتان في حائط المسجد الحرام لموضع الهرولة. وبعدما يرتفع من الوادي يمشي على هيئته إلى أن يأتي المروة فيصعد عليها ويفعل عليها ما فعل على الصفا من الدعاء والثناء وغيرهما، ثم ينزل منه ويذهب إلى الصفا، هكذا يفعل سبع مرات.
[٣] قوله: الأخضرين؛ فيه تغليب فإن الأخضر واحد منهما، والآخر أصفر.
[٤] قوله: شوط واحد؛ هذا القول نقله كثير من الفقهاء، وحكم بعض منهم بكونه غلطاً، وبعضهم قال: ليس بصحيح، ووجهه في «غاية البيان» بما لا يفيد ولا يغني.
ثم قال: وفي رواية الطحاوي السعي من الصفا إلى المروة، ثم منها إلى الصفا شوط واحد، فيكون أربعة عشر شوطاً على الرواية الثانية، ويقع الختم على الصفا. واستدل بقوله تعالى: {إِنَّ الصَّفَا وَالْمَرْوَةَ مِن شَعَائِرِ اللَّهِ فَمَنْ حَجَّ الْبَيْتَ أَوِ اعْتَمَرَ فَلَا جُنَاحَ عَلَيْهِ أَن يَطَّوَّفَ بِهِمَا}، أخرجه النسائي.
And he made takbir, tahmid, sent blessings upon the Prophet, peace be upon him, raised his hands, and supplicated as he wished. Then he walked towards Marwah, running between the two green markers, ascended it, and did what he did on Safa. He does this seven times, starting with Safa and ending with Marwah. Then the commentator said: [1] His saying: 'and raised his hands' i.e., for supplication, for it is recommended in every supplication. [2] His saying: 'then walked' i.e., he descends from Safa heading towards Marwah – which is a mountain – and walks normally, then runs between the two green markers in the valley bottom until he comes out of it. These two markers are the signs fixed in the wall of the Sacred Mosque for the place of hurried walking. After coming out of the valley, he walks normally until he reaches Marwah, ascends it, and does on it what he did on Safa of supplication, praise, etc. Then he descends and goes to Safa, doing this seven times. [3] His saying: 'the two green markers' involves predominance, for one of them is green and the other yellow. [4] His saying: 'one circuit'; this opinion was reported by many jurists, and some of them deemed it a mistake, while others said it is not correct. Its explanation in 'Ghayat al-Bayan' is not beneficial or sufficient. And he said: In al-Tahawi's narration, the sa’y from Safa to Marwah and then from it to Safa is one circuit, so it becomes fourteen circuits according to the second narration, and the conclusion occurs at Safa. He adduced the verse: {Indeed, Safa and Marwah are among the symbols of Allah. So whoever makes Hajj to the House or performs 'Umrah, there is no blame upon him to go between them}, narrated by al-Nasa'i.
Source pages p. 574

Commentary
This passage details the actions of sa'y. After completing tawaf and the prayer, the pilgrim proceeds to Safa, faces the Kaaba, makes takbir and tahmid, and recites blessings upon the Prophet ﷺ. He raises his hands in supplication. Then he walks towards Marwah, but in the valley between the two green markers (actually one green and one yellow), he runs (harwala). On reaching Marwah, he does the same acts. This back-and-forth is repeated seven times, starting at Safa and ending at Marwah. Each crossing (from one hill to the other) is one circuit. The text then discusses a dispute: some, like al-Tahawi, define the circuit as a full round from Safa to Marwah and back to Safa, which would make fourteen steps. The majority consider this a mistake. The relied-upon Hanafi position is seven circuits, one per crossing, ending at Marwah on the seventh.

Key Terms
شوط (shauṭ): In the context of sa'y, a single crossing from Safa to Marwah or from Marwah to Safa; the standard view counts seven such crossings.
الميل الأخضر (al-mīl al-akhḍar): A green marker in the Sacred Mosque indicating the start of the running area (harwala); actually one marker is green and the other yellow, but called 'the two green markers' by predominance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, how many circuits (ashwaat) are there in sa'y?
A) 7
B) 14
C) 4
D) 1

Key Takeaway
Sa'y in Hajj and Umrah is wajib and consists of seven one-way crossings between Safa and Marwah, ending at Marwah; the alternative definition of a circuit as a round trip is rejected.

The Performance of Sa'i between Safa and Marwah
Description of Sa'i with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the correct Hanafi procedure for performing Sa'i between Safa and Marwah and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the counting of laps.

Core Idea
Sa'i is seven one-way laps, starting at Safa and ending at Marwah, each direction counts as a lap.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah details the actions of Sa'i, including supplications, jogging between green markers, and the exact counting of laps. It also addresses an intra-Hanafi disagreement: whether each direction counts as a separate lap (seven total) or the round trip counts as one (fourteen total). The commentator firmly upholds the majority view, supported by the Quranic verse and the standard practice (Zahir al-Riwayah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the actions at Safa
Upon ascending Safa, the pilgrim says takbir, tahmid, salawat, raises hands, and supplicates. These are sunan (recommended acts).
These actions are repeated at Marwah as well.
2
Distinguish the two modes of walking
The pilgrim walks normally except between the two green markers (the valley floor), where he jogs (sa'i). After leaving the valley, normal walking resumes until Marwah.
The green markers are fixed in the mosque wall; one is green, the other yellow.
3
Understand the counting of laps (ashwat)
Each trip from Safa to Marwah is one shawt (lap), and each return from Marwah to Safa is another. Thus, seven laps total, starting at Safa and ending at Marwah.
This is the correct (sahih) view according to the commentator.
4
Evaluate the minority view
Al-Tahawi reported a narration considering the round trip (Safa to Marwah and back) as one shawt, yielding fourteen shawts and ending at Safa. The commentator rejects this as incorrect, citing the majority of Hanafi jurists.
The commentator states 'wa al-sahih huwa al-awwal' (the correct is the first).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكبر وهلل، وصلى على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ، ورفع يديه ودعا بما شاء ، ثم مشى نحو المروة ساعياً بين الميلين الأخضرين ، وصعد عليها ، وفعل ما فعله على الصفاء يفعل هكذا سَبْعاً، يبدأ بالصفا، ويختم بالمروة) أي السعي من الصفا إلى المروة شوط ، ثُمَّ من المروة إلى الصفا شوط آخر، فيكونُ بداية السعي من الصفا، وختمه، وهو السَّابع على المروة. وفي رواية الطَّحَاوِي السَّعي من الصَّفا إلى المروة، ثُمَّ منها إلى الصفا شوط واحد ، فيكون أربعة عشر شوطاً على الرواية الثانية ، ويقع الختم على الصفا. إِنَّ الصَّفَا وَالْمَرْوَةَ مِن شَعَابِرِ اللَّهِ فَمَنْ حَجَّ الْبَيْتَ أَوِ اعْتَمَرَ فَلَا جُنَاحَ عَلَيْهِ أَن يَطَّوَفَ بِهِمَا، أخرجه النَّسَائي. قوله : ورفع يديه؛ أي للدعاء، فإنه يستحب في كل دعاء. قوله : ثم مشى؛ أي ينزل من الصفا ذاهباً إلى جانب المروة – وهو بالفتح : جبل -، ويمشي على هيئته، ويسعى بين الميلين الأخضرين في بطن الوادي إلى أن يرتفع منه، وهما العلامتان المركوزان في حائط المسجد الحرام لموضع الهرولة. وبعدما يرتفع من الوادي يمشي على هيئته إلى أن يأتي المروة فيصعد عليها ويفعل عليها ما فعل على الصفا من الدعاء والثناء وغيرهما، ثم يَنْزِلُ منه ويذهب إلى الصفا، هكذا يفعل سبع مرات. قوله : الأخضرين؛ فيه تغليب فإنّ الأخضر واحد منهما، والآخر أصفر. قوله : شوط واحد؛ هذا القولُ نقله كثير من الفقهاء، وحكم بعض منهم بكونه غلطاً، وبعضهم قال : ليس بصحيح، ووجهه في «غاية البيان» بما لا يفيد ولا يغني. لكن اختار الطحاوي في مختصره رواية أن من الصفا إلى المروة شوط، فقال: فيفعل عليها – أي المروة – كما يفعل على الصفا حتى يفعل ذلك سبع مرات يبتدئ في كل مرة منها بالصفا ويختم بالمروة. البقرة: من الآية ١٥٨. والصحيح هو الأول.
And he said Allahu Akbar, and said La ilaha illallah, and sent blessings upon the Prophet, and raised his hands and supplicated as he wished, then walked towards Marwah, jogging between the two green markers, and ascended it, and did what he did on Safa. He does this seven times, starting at Safa and ending at Marwah. That is, the sa'i from Safa to Marwah is one lap, then from Marwah to Safa is another lap, so the beginning of sa'i is at Safa, and its conclusion, which is the seventh, is at Marwah. And in the narration of al-Tahawi: the sa'i from Safa to Marwah, then from it to Safa is one lap, so there are fourteen laps according to the second narration, and the conclusion occurs at Safa. "Indeed, Safa and Marwah are among the symbols of Allah. So whoever makes Hajj to the House or performs 'Umrah, there is no blame upon him to walk between them." Al-Nasa'i recorded it. The commentator says: "And he raised his hands" i.e., for supplication, for it is recommended in every supplication. "Then walked" i.e., he descends from Safa heading towards the side of Marwah – which is a mountain – and walks at his normal pace, and jogs between the two green markers in the valley floor until he rises from it, and they are the two markers fixed in the wall of the Sacred Mosque for the place of rapid walking. After he rises from the valley, he walks at his normal pace until he reaches Marwah, then ascends it and does on it what he did on Safa: supplication, praise, and other things. Then he descends from it and goes to Safa. He does this seven times. "The two green markers" involves a predominance, for one of them is green and the other is yellow. "One lap" – this statement many jurists have narrated, and some have judged it to be a mistake, and some said it is not correct, and its explanation in Ghayat al-Bayan is not beneficial nor sufficient. However, al-Tahawi in his Mukhtasar chose the narration that from Safa to Marwah is one lap, so he said: do on it (i.e., Marwah) as you did on Safa, until you do that seven times, starting each time at Safa and ending at Marwah. Surah al-Baqarah 2:158. And the correct is the first.
Source pages p. 574

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) of the description of the sa'i ritual. It opens by detailing the actions: after the rituals on Safa, the pilgrim says Allahu Akbar, praises Allah, sends blessings on the Prophet, raises hands in supplication, then walks towards Marwah. Between the two green markers (fixed in the wall of the mosque indicating the valley floor), he engages in rapid walking (sa'i), then after ascending Marwah, he repeats the same supplications and praise. This constitutes one lap; he does seven laps total, starting at Safa and ending at Marwah. The commentator clarifies that each movement from Safa to Marwah is one shawt, and the return from Marwah to Safa is another shawt. The beginning is at Safa, and the seventh lap ends at Marwah. Then the commentator reports an alternative narration from Imam al-Tahawi, who considered a single trip from Safa to Marwah as one shawt, resulting in fourteen shawts (seven round trips) and ending at Safa. The commentator rejects this, citing the Quranic verse (2:158) that mentions 'walking between them' and concluding that the correct view is the first: seven laps starting and ending as described. The footnotes explain details: raising hands is for supplication; 'then walked' means descending Safa toward Marwah; the two green markers are fixed in the mosque wall for the spot of hurrying. After the valley, normal walking resumes until Marwah. The term 'al-akhdarayn' (two green) involves taghlib, as one is green and the other yellow. Finally, the commentator dismisses the minority view and reaffirms the mashhur position.

Key Terms
الصفا (al-Safa): A hill near the Kaaba in Mecca, from which the Sa'i ritual begins.
المروة (al-Marwah): Another hill, where the Sa'i ritual ends.
السعي (al-Sa'i): The ritual walking or jogging between Safa and Marwah seven times.
الميلين الأخضرين (al-milayn al-akhḍarayn): Two markers in the mosque, one green and one yellow (predominantly called green), indicating the area where jogging (ramal) is performed.
شوط (shawṭ): One segment of Sa'i from one hill to the other; a lap.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many laps (ashwat) does a pilgrim perform in Sa'i according to the correct Hanafi position?
A) Fourteen laps (each direction counted as one lap)
B) Seven laps (from Safa to Marwah or Marwah to Safa counted as a lap)
C) Seven laps (only Safa to Marwah counts as a lap, return is not counted)
D) Three laps

Key Takeaway
The correct Hanafi practice is seven one-way laps between Safa and Marwah, starting at Safa and ending at Marwah, with each direction counted as a separate lap.

Actions of a Mufrid after Sa'i: Residence, Tawaf, and Sermons
Expansion on the Mufrid's actions after sa'i and the Imam's sermons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings for a Mufrid after sa'i: residence in ihram, voluntary tawaf, prohibition of voluntary sa'i, and the sunnah status of the three sermons.

Core Idea
A Mufrid remains in ihram, may perform voluntary tawaf, but must not repeat sa'i; the Imam's three sermons are sunnah.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah expands on the actions of a Mufrid (one performing Hajj only) after completing the initial tawaf and sa'i. It clarifies that the pilgrim stays in Mecca in a state of ihram, may engage in voluntary tawaf, but should not repeat sa'i voluntarily. Additionally, it describes the three sermons delivered by the Imam on the 7th, 9th, and 11th of Dhul-Hijjah, all of which are sunnah. Understanding these rulings ensures the pilgrim follows the sunnah and avoids innovations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Mufrid's state after sa'i
The Mufrid remains in ihram and resides in Mecca; the word 'sakana' (resides) is used rather than 'aqama' because a formal residence is not required.
He does not remove ihram until after completing all Hajj rituals.
2
Determine the ruling on voluntary tawaf
The Mufrid may perform voluntary tawaf as much as he wishes; it is recommended (sunnah) and not restricted.
This is unlike the initial tawaf which was a greeting (tahiyyah).
3
Understand why voluntary sa'i is not allowed
Sa'i is wajib only once for the Mufrid; repeating it voluntarily is not legislated (ghayr mashru'), unlike tawaf.
The source cites al-Hidayah for this ruling.
4
Recognize the three sermons and their timing
The Imam delivers sermons on the 7th (after zuhr), 9th (at Arafat before zuhr), and 11th (after zuhr) of Dhul-Hijjah.
The sermon on the 9th is distinct: it is before zuhr and consists of one sitting without a break.
5
Classify the hukm of the sermons
All three sermons are sunnah (recommended), not wajib, as stated in Lubab al-Manasik.
Some other schools may consider the Arafah sermon as wajib, but the Hanafi position is sunnah.

ثم سكن بمكة مُحرماً، وطافَ بالبيتِ نفلاً ما شاء، وخطب الإمام سابع ذي الحجة، وعَلَّمَ فيها المناسك، ثم التاسع بعرفات، ثم الحادي عشر بمنى. قوله : ثم سكن؛ أي المفرد بالحج بعدما فرغ من الطواف والسعي، ولم يقل أقام؛ لأن الإقامة الشرعية لا يلزم وجودها. قوله : محرماً؛ لكونه محرماً بالحج، فلا يتحلّل من الإحرام إلا بعد الفراغ من الحج، وما سبق من الطواف كان تحية للقدوم. قوله : نفلاً ما شاء؛ أي عدد شاء؛ لأنه عبادة، فيستحب الإكثار منها، ولا يمنع منها في حال من الإحوال إلا بوجه شرعي، ولا سعي بعد هذه الأطوفة؛ لأنّ السعي لا يجب في المفرد بالحج إلا مرة واحدة، والتنفّل بالسعي غير مشروع. كذا في «الهداية». قوله : سابع ذي الحجة؛ أي بعد الزوال وبعد صلاة الظهر، وكُره قبله كما في «السراج»، وهذه الخطبة واحدة من غير جلسة، ذكره العيني. قوله : وعلم؛ من التعليم: أي ذكر فيها مناسك الحج وأفعاله. قوله : ثم التاسع؛ أي ثمّ يخطب في يوم عرفة بعرفات بعد الزوال قبل صلاة الظهر، ثمّ في اليوم الحادي عشر، وهي أيضاً كالأولى أو بعد الظهر خطبة واحدة بلا جلسة بخلافِ خطبة عرفة، وكلّ من الخطب الثلاث سنّة. كذا في «لباب المناسك».
Then he resides in Mecca in the state of ihram, and performs voluntary tawaf of the House as much as he wishes. The Imam delivers a sermon on the seventh of Dhul-Hijjah and teaches the rites in it, then on the ninth at Arafat, then on the eleventh at Mina. The commentator says: "Then he resides" meaning the one performing Hajj individually (Mufrid) after finishing the tawaf and sa'i; he did not say "he stays" because the legal stay (iqamah) is not necessarily present. "In ihram" because he is in ihram for Hajj, so he does not remove his ihram until after completing Hajj; the previous tawaf was a greeting tawaf (tahiyyah). "Voluntarily as he wishes" meaning any number he wishes, because it is worship, and it is recommended to perform many, and he is not prevented from it in any situation except for a legal reason. There is no sa'i after these tawafs, because sa'i is obligatory for the Mufrid only once, and performing voluntary sa'i is not legislated. This is from al-Hidayah. "Seventh of Dhul-Hijjah" meaning after zawal (noon) and after Zuhr prayer, and it is disliked before as in al-Siraj; this sermon is one without a sitting, as mentioned by al-Ayni. "And teaches" from teaching, meaning he mentions in it the rituals and actions of Hajj. "Then the ninth" meaning he gives a sermon on the Day of Arafah at Arafat after zawal before Zuhr prayer, then on the eleventh day; it is also like the first: one sermon without a sitting, unlike the sermon of Arafah. All three sermons are sunnah. This is from Lubab al-Manasik.
Source pages p. 575

Commentary
This passage expands on the actions of a Mufrid (one performing Hajj only, without Umrah) after completing tawaf al-qudum (arrival tawaf) and sa'i. He then resides in Mecca in a state of ihram; the commentator notes that the word 'sakana' (resides) is used rather than 'aqama' (stays) because the legal concept of iqamah (settling) is not required. He remains in ihram until after Hajj. He may perform voluntary tawaf as much as he wishes, since it is worship; however, he should not repeat sa'i because sa'i is wajib only once for the Mufrid. The Imam delivers three sermons: on the 7th of Dhul-Hijjah (after zuhr, one sermon without sitting), on the 9th at Arafat (before zuhr), and on the 11th at Mina (after zuhr). These sermons are sunnah; the one on Arafah is distinguished by being before zuhr and consisting of one sermon without a sitting in between (unlike the Friday sermon). The passage emphasizes that voluntary sa'i is not prescribed, and the sermons teach the rites of Hajj.

Key Terms
المفرد بالحج (al-Mufrid bi al-Hajj): One who performs Hajj only, without combining it with Umrah.
خطبة (khutbah): A sermon delivered by the Imam during the days of Hajj, specifically on the 7th, 9th, and 11th of Dhul-Hijjah.
تطوع بالسعي (tatawwu' bi al-sa'i): Performing voluntary sa'i, which is not legislated according to Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on voluntary sa'i for a Mufrid (one performing Hajj only)?
A) Recommended just like voluntary tawaf
B) Permissible but disliked
C) Not legislated (ghayr mashru')
D) Obligatory

Key Takeaway
The Mufrid may perform voluntary tawaf but not sa'i; the Imam's three sermons are sunnah.

Hajj — Pilgrimage
intermediate
8 min
Hajj of a Mufrid: Optional Tawaf, Three Khutbahs, and Departure to Mina
Commentary expanding the Hajj sequence for a mufrid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the three Khutbahs and the stay in Mina for a mufrid, and identify the proof from the Sunnah.

Core Idea
The Prophet's practice makes the three Khutbahs and the Mina stay Sunnah Muakkadah for the mufrid.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies the recommended actions for a pilgrim performing Hajj alone (mufrid) after the arrival tawaf. It explains the permissibility of numerous optional tawaf, the timing of the Imam's three sermons, and the journey to Mina on the 8th. We will follow the commentator's exposition to understand the basis and classification of these acts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actions ruled upon
List the actions: staying in Mecca as muhrim, performing optional tawaf, three Khutbahs (7th, 9th, 11th), and departing to Mina on Yawm al-Tarwiyah and staying there.
These are part of the Hajj sequence for a mufrid.
2
Determine the hukm level
From the commentary, optional tawaf are nafl; the three Khutbahs and the stay in Mina are Sunnah Muakkadah. The Hanafi school classifies these based on the source's indication.
Not obligatory (wajib), as clarified by the commentator.
3
Locate the dalil
The passage cites the hadith of Jabir in Sahih Muslim that the Prophet (SAW) prayed Fajr on the 8th in Makkah, went to Mina, prayed all five prayers there, and proceeded to Arafat on the 9th.
This is the primary evidence for the recommended sequence.
4
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
In Hanafi usul, the Prophet's action (fi'l) indicates recommendation (Sunnah) unless there is a command or context suggesting obligation. Here, no such command is present.
Thus the ruling is Sunnah, not wajib.
5
Note the practice for the mufrid
The mufrid remains in ihram throughout and can perform as many optional tawaf as desired, but sa'i is only required once.
Optional tawaf are preferred over supererogatory prayer for non-residents.

Book of Hajj, Chapter of Ihram. Then he (the pilgrim) resided in Mecca as a Muhrim, and circumambulated the House optionally as much as he wished. And the Imam delivered a sermon on the seventh of Dhul-Hijjah, and taught therein the rituals. Then the ninth at Arafat, then the eleventh at Mina. And the correct is the first. (Then he resided in Mecca as a Muhrim, and circumambulated the House optionally as much as he wished). And the Imam delivered a sermon on the seventh of Dhul-Hijjah, and taught therein the rituals: which are going out to Mina, prayer at Arafat, and the ifada. Then the ninth at Arafat, then the eleventh at Mina. [1] His saying: 'then he resided' means the one performing Hajj individually (mufrid) after finishing tawaf and sa'i. He did not say 'he stayed' because legal residence (iqama) is not necessarily required. [2] His saying: 'as a Muhrim' because he is in a state of ihram for Hajj, so he does not exit ihram until after finishing Hajj. The previous tawaf was a greeting of arrival. [3] His saying: 'optionally as much as he wished' meaning any number he wishes, because it is an act of worship, so increasing it is recommended, and one is not prevented from it under any circumstance except for a legal reason. And there is no sa'i after these circumambulations because sa'i is only obligatory for the mufrid once, and performing optional sa'i is not legislated. Thus in al-Hidayah. [4] His saying: 'the seventh of Dhul-Hijjah' meaning after zawal (declination of the sun) and after the zuhr prayer, and it is disliked before as in al-Siraj. And this is one sermon without a sitting, mentioned by al-Ayni. [0] His saying: 'and taught' from teaching, meaning he mentioned therein the rituals of Hajj and its actions. [6] His saying: 'then the ninth' meaning then he delivers a sermon on the day of Arafah at Arafat after zawal before zuhr prayer, then on the eleventh day, and it is also like the first, or after zuhr, one sermon without a sitting, contrary to the sermon of Arafah. And all three sermons are sunnah. Thus in Lubab al-Manāsik. (1) Al-Kasani corrected it in al-Bada'i (2:135) and others. (2) Without ramal and without sa'i, and because it is worship, it is better than prayer for strangers, and he prays after each week. See: Majma' al-Anhur (1:274). (3) Al-Hidayah (2:465). (4) Lubab al-Manāsik (pp. 205-206). He separates between each two sermons by a day. Then he departed on the morning of the day of Tarwiyah to Mina, and stayed there until the dawn of the day of Arafah. He separates between each two sermons by a day. Then he departed on the morning of the day of Tarwiyah, which is the eighth of Dhul-Hijjah, so called because they water the camels on this day, to Mina, and stayed there until the dawn of the day of Arafah. [1] His saying: 'he separates' means he makes the separation a day; for the first is on the seventh, the second on the ninth, and the third on the eleventh. [2] His saying: 'then he departed on the morning' with fatha (ghadat) meaning after the Fajr prayer of the eighth of Dhul-Hijjah. [3] His saying: 'because they' meaning the owners of camels used to water the camels from water on this day in preparation for standing on the ninth at Arafah, since there was no running water at Arafat at that time, as in our time. [4] His saying: 'to Mina' – with kasr of the mim – a place within the Haram about a farsakh from Mecca. [0] His saying: 'and stayed' meaning he halted in Mina on that day and the night of the ninth. And it is established in Sahih Muslim and others that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) prayed Fajr on the day of Tarwiyah in Mecca, and when the sun rose he went to Mina, and prayed there Zuhr, Asr, Maghrib, Isha, and Fajr on the day of Arafah, then he went to Arafat. (2) (1) Mina: a village where sacrificial animals are slaughtered; it is named Mina because of the decrees of death (mana ya) upon the animals. And it is a distance of one farsakh from the Haram. See: Talibat al-Talaba (p.31), al-Durr al-Mukhtar (2:172), al-Maqadir al-Shar'iyya (p.208). (2) From Jabir: When it was the day of Tarwiyah they set out to Mina and pronounced talbiyah for Hajj, and the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) rode and prayed there Zuhr, Asr, Maghrib, Isha, and Fajr, then stayed a little until the sun rose and ordered a tent of hair to be set up for him at Namira... and he found the tent had been set up for him at Namira and he dismounted there. In Sahih Muslim (2:889) and Sahih Ibn Hibban (4:310) and others.
Source pages p. 575

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of al-Wiqaya, explaining the sequence of Hajj for a mufrid (one performing Hajj only). It opens with the ruling that after completing the arrival tawaf and sa'i, the pilgrim resides in Mecca as a muhrim and may perform as many optional tawaf as he wishes, since it is a recommended act of worship. The commentator clarifies that the term 'sakan' (resided) is used instead of 'aqama' (stayed) because legal residence (iqama shar'iyya) is not required. The pilgrim remains in ihram until after completing all Hajj rites. Optional tawaf are allowed without ramal or sa'i, and they are superior to supererogatory prayer for strangers (non-residents of Makkah). The commentator then explains the three Khutbahs delivered by the Imam: on the 7th of Dhul-Hijjah (after zawal, before zuhr if possible, otherwise after zuhr), teaching the rituals; on the 9th (Day of Arafah) at Arafat; and on the 11th at Mina. Each Khutbah is a single sermon without a sitting, and all are Sunnah. The Khutbahs are separated by a full day: 7th, 9th, 11th. Next, the commentary covers the departure on the morning of the 8th (Yawm al-Tarwiyah) to Mina, staying there until after Fajr of the 9th. The name 'Tarwiyah' is derived from the practice of watering camels in preparation for Arafat. The commentator cites the hadith from Sahih Muslim that the Prophet (SAW) prayed Fajr in Makkah on the 8th, went to Mina, prayed all five prayers there, and then proceeded to Arafat on the 9th. This serves as the primary proof (dalil naqli) for the recommended practice. The commentator notes that Mina is a place within the Haram about one farsakh (approx. 5.04 km) from Makkah. The entire passage emphasizes the specific sequence and its basis in the Sunnah.

Key Terms
مُحْرِم (muhrim): A person who has entered the state of ihram for Hajj or Umrah, with specific prohibitions.
نَفْل (nafl): Supererogatory or optional act of worship, here specifically optional tawaf around the Kaaba.
يَوْم التَّرْوِيَة (Yawm al-Tarwiyah): The 8th day of Dhul-Hijjah; named because pilgrims used to water their camels in preparation for the day at Arafat.
غَدَاة (ghadah (or ghadat)): Early morning, after Fajr prayer but before sunrise; the time for departing to Mina on the 8th.
مِنًى (Mina): A place within the Haram boundaries, about one farsakh (approx. 5 km) from Makkah, where pilgrims stay on the 8th, 11th, 12th, and sometimes 13th of Dhul-Hijjah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
On which day does the Imam deliver the second Khutbah according to this passage?
A) The 7th of Dhul-Hijjah at Makkah
B) The 8th of Dhul-Hijjah at Mina
C) The 9th of Dhul-Hijjah at Arafat
D) The 11th of Dhul-Hijjah at Mina

Key Takeaway
For a mufrid, the three Imam's Khutbahs (7th, 9th, 11th) and the stay in Mina on the 8th are Sunnah Muakkadah, based on the Prophet's practice as recorded in Sahih Muslim.
